Dairy Farms                           7%         3           5           8
"fixed Farming                        7%          1           7          8
Date Palm Plantation                       6/%         8           7          15
Agro-industries                            8%          2           5           7
Detailed terms and conditions of sub-loans and investment grants to small
farmers and SCMUVs vary according to the type of investment and are summarized
in Annex 9 Table 1.



- 25 -
6.12      BNT would carry 100% of the default risk of outstanding amounts on
loans to all subborrowers other than small farmers and small farmers associa-
tions (SCMVs). For small farmers and SCMVs, BNT would be onlending its own
funds in blend with Bank funds but will have partial recourse to Government
in the case of non-repayment of loan installments one year after due. BNT
would assume 10% of the default risk on sub-loans granted during the first
two-year period following Bank loan effectiveness and 25% later on. The
smaller proportion of risk to be borne by BNT on sub-loans to small farmers
and SCMVs, categories of subborrowers considered by BNT as less credit-worthy,
would encourage BNT to undertake medium term lending to these subborrowers and
to follow-up more closely sub-project appraisal and supervision. BNT would
also play a more active role in agricultural development, a sector where BNT's
activity and direct involvement has so far been very limited. The phased
assumption of risk will also provide the opportunity for BNT to develop the
procedures and staffing required for it to assume risk on small farmer lending.
Even the partial assumption of risk by BNT on small farmer credit is expected
to result in improved repayment performance; current FOSDA loan repayment on
which BNT takes no risk is only 65% of dues while BNT collects 92% of dues on
its own funds lent to farmers. Increasing the level of risk assumption by
BNT on small farmer lending is expected to continue and Government intends to
have BNT and all lending institutions in Tunisia gradually assume the repayment
risk for all lending, including FOSDA.
6.13      Government would periodically reimburse BNT the differential between
the on-lending rates and the Bank rate of 8.7%. In addition Government would
pay to BNT a compensatory commission equal to 3% of the portion of outstanding
loans financed by the Bank. This commission would cover BNT's Project related
administrative expenses and would enable BNT to set up an adequate provision
for bad debts.
6.14      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the Lending
Policies and Procedures outlined in paras 6.03-6.13 would be applied for all
lending under the Project, and that no loans for purposes similar to those of
the Project on terms more advantageous would be made available to subborrowers.
C. Monitoring of Development Results
6.15      In order to improve lending policies investment results at the sub-
project level would be monitored on a representative sample basis. BNT would
set up a mechanism for this purpose not later than 12 months after effective-
ness of the loan. Suggested procedures would be submitted by BNT to the Bank
for approval within 9 months after effectiveness. Assurances to this effect
were obtained during negotiations.



- 26 -
D. Accounts and Audits
6.16      BNT has a good accounting organization as well as a management
information system. BNT is also subject to the customary supervision of banks
by the Central Bank, and Government appoints a Financial Controller (Controleur
Financier) to protect its interest in BNT. External financial control is
carried out by an independent controller (Commissaire aux Comptes) appointed
by the shareholders' General Assembly. During appraisal of the First Agricul-
tural Credit Project, this external financial control had been found unsatis-
factory and Bank had obtained during negotiations assurances that BNT's
accounts would be audited by an independent auditor acceptable to the Bank.
BNT complied with this condition in 1971, but beginning in 1972, the quality
of external auditing deteriorated and became unsatisfactory. Bank staff have
discussed this with BNT management and the Bank obtained formal assurances
during negotiations that auditing will be done by an independent auditor
acceptable to Bank, and that the Audit Report will be submitted to the Bank
and the Board of BNT, not later than 6 months after the end of the fiscal
year. Due to efforts to improve the 1975 audit the report is not expected to
be received until the end of the year. It would be condition of effectiveness
that audit for year 1975 has been completed and submitted to Bank.
VII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATIONS
A. Production and Marketing
Production
7.01      Investment under the Project is expected to generate the following
incremental production at full development:
Quantity               Value
tons                 D'000
Grain                                             13,155                  710
Fruit and Vegetable                              22,700                 1,350
Milk                                               6,500                  585
Beef                                                 370                  125
Oxen                                                 225 heads             80
In-Calf Heifers                                      115 heads             45
Dates                                              3,000 tons             540
Olive Oil                                          5,357 tons           3,241 /1
Total                                                                 6,676
/1   Including olive cake.



Mlarket ing
7.02      No difficulty is expected for marketing of all agriculture products
except olive oil. Tunisia is a net importer of grain (320,000 tons in year
1974/75) and the generated incremental production would barely cover 4.1% of
the present imports. Fruit and vegetable production financed under the Proj-
ect would also be consumed locally, principally in the main urban centers such
as Tunis, Bizerte, Sousse and Sfax and no marketing problems and difficulties
are to be expected. Tunisia's milk production in 1975 amounted to about
260,000 tons and had to be complemented by imports (in powder form) of 150,000
tons equivalent of whole milk. The production deficit is expected to be even
higher in 1976 and present milk production would have to be doubled between
1975 and 1980 to meet the projected consumption figure for 1980. Beef produc-
tion in 1975 amounted to about 95,000 tons and some 5,000 tons had to be im-
ported. Consumption is expected to reach 134,000 tons by 1980, an increase
of 35% as compared to the consumption figure for 1975. The Project's incre-
mental production would cover only about 1% of the consumption increase. No
marketing difficulties are therefore to be expected either for milk and milk
products or for beef.
7.03      The Project generated date production at full development would be
entirely dates of the Deglat variety which are essentially for export. How-
ever, only the top qualities are exported so that 50% of the produced quantity
or about 1,500 tons would be available for export. The quality of the Deglat
variety is highly praised on the European market and quantities presently
exported (average 1972-74, 5,800 tons) constitute only a fraction of the
European imports. An increase in Deglat exports of 1,500 tons should in no
way modify this relation.
7.04      In 1975 Tunisia olive oil exports encountered substantial difficul-
ties due on one hand to an excellent harvest on both sides of the M4editer-
ranean basin and on the other hand to new import restrictions on the part of
the European Community. An agreement signed in January 1976 by Tunisia and
EEC provides for a reduction of import duties on olive oil from US$570 equiv-
alent per ton to US$420 equivalent per ton which, at current prices, re-
presents an import levy of respectively 50% and 30%.  This reduction should
substantially facilitate Tunisia's olive oil export; however, an effort based
both on improvements of quality and presentation and on diversification of
markets will be needed to overcome possible future difficulties should the
present convention not be renewed.
B. Financial Benefits
7.05      The financial results for typical investment models under the Proj-
ect are given in Annex 5. The calculations are based on Tunisian prices for
the second half of 1975 for both inputs and outputs. The financial rates of
return of the models vary between 11% (Model 7: Date Palm Plantations) and
over 50% (Model 1: 5 ha Fruit and Vegetable Farm). The sensitivity analysis



- 28 -
vields the following: if investment cost were raised by 10%, the financial
rates of return would vary between 8% and over 50%; an increase in operating
expenses of 10% would result in financial rates of return between 11% and over
50%; and if revenues were to drop by 10% the rates would range from 4% to over
50%.
Economic Rate of Return
7.06      The economy is expected to realize a return of 34% from total proj-
ect investments, including physical contingencies (Annex 8). The economic
rate of return for each component of the Project is expected to be 38% for
Farm Development, 13% for Date Plantations, 36% for Oil Mills. The economic
rate of return on the farm development component is higher than what the rate
of return on the First Agricultural Credit Project (excluding date plantations)
is likely to be because a number of small subborrowers are expected to invest
in new wells, small scale irrigation and fruit and vegetable crops which have
a high economic and financial rate of return. No such subprojects were
financed under the first Credit Project. Hired labor has been valued at
prevailing wage rates. Family labor has been costed at 50% because of limited
employment opportunities on farms affected by the Project. Grain, livestock
products and fertilizers have been valued at projected world market prices.
Other commodities and inputs have been valued at farm-gate prices adjusted for
import duties, taxes and subsidies. A sensitivity analysis was applied to
test the Project against changes in investment costs, operating expenses and
production results. By increasing investment costs and operating expenses
alternatively by 10% the rate of return for the whole Project would drop to
29% under either assumption. By decreasing the value of the benefits by 10%,
the overall return would drop to 23% (Annex 8).
Project Beneficiaries and Income Increases
7.07      About 935 private farmers, 4 SCMVs with a total membership of about
300 and 18 oil mills would benefit from the Project. Of the 1,235 benefi-
ciaries, about 960 (i.e., more than three quarters) would be small farmers,
including all the SCMV members. They would receive 48% of the Bank loan,
accounting for 53% of the total investment cost. About 475 farmers, including
most of the SCMV members (or about 38% of the beneficiaries), can be defined
as belonging to the rural poverty target group (para 6.04). At full develop-
ment (2-5 years for investments other than orchards and plantations) the small
farmer beneficiaries would have an increase in farm income from a maximum of
D 600 to an average of D 1.578. About 280 farmers and the 18 oil millowners
would belong to the class of medium and large landowniers and/or businessmen.
The income of farmers in this group would be expected to increase from a
medium between D 2,000-D 2,500 to a medium between D 4,500-D 6,000 with full
development of their project investments.



- 29 -
C. Economic Benefits
Employment
7.08      The Project would generate substantial employment opportunities.
An estimated 1,300 man years of additional family labor would be generated
from investments in farm development, thus reducing underemployment on the
935 farms at an investment cost of D 4,200 (US$10,000) per job created (in
1975 prices). In addition, 300 man years of additional family labor would
be generated from investments in date plantations at an investment cost of
D 5,300 (US$12,700) and 350 jobs would be created in the oil mills at a cost
per job of D 5,000 (US$12,000).
7.09      There would also be sizeable employment opportunities generated
through the additional investments, the additional consumption of inputs and
the increased production, via backward and forward linkages. These employ-
ment opportunities have not been quantified. The additional foreign exchange
earnings attributable to the Project would not be significant, most of the
production being consumed locally.  The improvement in the quality of ex-
ported olive oil and resulting higher prices and higher foreign exchange
earnings have not been quantified.
Other Benefits
7.10      The Project would further result in non-quantifiable institutional
benefits affecting both agriculture credit in general and BNT in particular:
(i) Although the Project itself would directly reach only a limited
number of the 85% of the Tunisian farmers not having access to
institutional credit, it would free other governmental funds
likely to be used for additional loans to small farmers pre-
viously deprived of access to credit ("ripple" effect).
(ii)  The Project would strongly support the present trend toward
an improvement in agricultural credit terms (clearer defini-
tion of target groups, higher and more coherent interest rates,
better coordination between various institutions and programs).
(iii)  Finally, the Project would support institutional building withLn
BNT and encourage it to become more involved in lending to small
farmers and to assure more of an agricultural development role.
VIIt.  AGREEMENTS REACHED AID RECOtMENDATIONS
8. 1      To insure successful project implementation the following major
assurances were obtained during negotiations:



- 30 -
(a)  BNT would maintain its existing Project Unit, increasing
staffing by one engineer qualified in agro-industries and
by additional personnel required to adequately evaluate
loan applications and supervise subprojects (para 6.02);
(b)  Subprojects would be monitored on a representative sample
basis. A mechanism and monitoring procedure would be pre-
pared by BNT not later than 9 months after Project effec-
tiveness and would be submitted to the Bank for comments
(para 6.15);
8.02      Conditions of effectiveness of the proposed Bank loan would be (a)
that audit of BNT's accounts for 1975 has been completed and auditor's report
submitted to Bank (para 6.16) and (b) that policies and procedures outlined
in paras 6.03-6.13 would be implemented for all FOSDA lending (para 6.14).
8.03      The Project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$12 million
equivalent to BNT for a term of 12 years with 4 years of grace.



ANINEX 1
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
TUNISIA
Agricultural Sector
General
1.        Tunisia has a total area of about 164,000 km2 and a population of
5.6 million with an annual growth rate of 2.3%. About 9 million ha is agri-
cultural land of which only about 3 million ha are currently cultivated and
110,000 ha are irrigated.  Agriculture provides about 18% of GDP, and employs
over 50% of the active population.  Exports of agricultural products amounted
in 1974 to D 100 million representing some 25% of Tunisia's total exports.
2.         From an agricultural point of view Tunisia can be divided into
three distinct regions:
(i) The North (4 million ha) comprising the North and North-
west of the country and Cap Bon. About 60% of the popu-
lation lives in this region, the land is of good quality
and rainfall is adequate reaching 500-1,500 mm during 70-
100 days per year; the region has over 3,500 farms of over
100 ha; irrigated areas cover 80,000 ha. About I million
ha are planted with cereals, practically all of the coun-
try's citrus orchards are located in the area, principally
in Cap Bon; about 30% of the total number of olive trees
are in the North, and almost all the vineyards. The
principal tovwns are Tunis, Bizerte, Beja, Jendouba, le
Kef Nabeul and Siliana, each of them the seat of a
Governorate.
(ii) The Center (6 million ha), has mainly pasture and grazing
lands.  Rainfall is limited to between 200 mm and 350 mm
for 40 to 70 days per year; apart from extensive livestock
raising, the main crop is olives. Around the main towns
vegetables are produced on about 13,000 ha. The principal
urban centers are Sousse, Mahdia, Kairouan and Kasserine.
(iii) The South (6 million ha), comprising the Governorates of
Sidi-Bouzid, Gafsa, Sfax, Gabes and Hiedenine, has a semi-
desertic climate and arid soils. Agricultural production
is concentrated in the oases which produce all of Tunisia's
date crop, and also some vegetables and fodder crops. Olive
trees are cultivated in the less arid areas, but yields per
ha are several times lower than in the other regions.



ANNEX I
Page 2
3.        Whereas the overall growth of the Tunisian economy during the decade
1961-70 amounted to over 60%, the value of agricultural production in constant
1966 prices increased only by 20% due to unfavorable climatic conditions and
the failure of the Government imposed cooperative system. During the first
lhalf of the present decade GDP in constant terms increased by over 65% and the
value of agricultural production in 1974, admittedly an exceptionally favor-
able year, was more than double that of 1970.
Institutional Framework
4.        Ministry of Agriculture.  The activities of the Ministry of Agri-
culture are divided over a number of departments (directions). Direction des
Grands Travaux Hydrauliques (major water-supply works), Direction du Genie
Rural (rural engineering), Direction des Resources en Eau et en Sol (water
and land resources), Direction de la Production Agricole (agricultural pro-
duction), Direction de la Recherche, de l'Enseignement et de la Formation
Professionnelle (research, education and training), Direction des Peches
(fisheries), Direction des Forets (forestry), Direction des Services Vete-
rinaries (veterinary services), Direction des Affaires Foncieres et Legisla-
tives (real estate and legislative affairs) and Direction des Services Admi-
nistratifs (administrative services).
5.        There are also two State Secretariats:  Secretariat d'Etat des
Resources Hydrauliques et du Secteur Irrigue (water resources and irrigated
sector), and Secretariat d'Etat de l'Agriculture en Sec (rainfed agriculture);
two councils: Conseil Superieur de la Recherche (Higher Research Council),
Conseil Superieur des Peches (Higher Fisheries Council); two commissions:
Commission des Marches (Markets Commission), Commission d'Etude et d'Agre-
ment des Projets (Projects Study and Approval Commission); and one committee:
Comite Consultatif du Credit et de 1'Encouragement (Consultative Committee
for Credit and Promotion). Finally at Cabinet level an Inspector General
for Agriculture, a Planning and Project Evaluation Department, a National
Center for Agricultural Studies and a Financing, Credit and Cooperation
Department.
6.        A number of semi-autonomous organizations and agencies are in charge
of specialized activities. Subject to some central Government auditing these
organizations are autonomous in financial and management matters.
The principal agencies are:
(a)  National Edible Oil Agency (Office National de l'Huile ONH),
(b)  National Grain Agency (Office National des Cereales ONC),
(c) National Wine Agency (Office National des Vins - ONV),
(d) National Livestock and Pasture Agency (Office National de
l'Elevage et des Paturages ONEP), and
(e) National Fisheries Agency (Office National des Peches - ONP).



ANNEX I
Page 3
7.        The first three were intended to be essentiallv marketing organiza-
tions with the marketing monopoly of the commodities concerned but have since
acquired extensions and credit channelling functions. ONEP has a broader
responsibilitv including in addition production and extension, import and
export regulations of livestock and animal feed.
8.        Other semi-public entities are in charge of the agricultural devel-
opment of a given region. To this category belongs the Miedjerda Valley
Development Authority (Office de Mise en Valeur de la Vallee de Medierda -
OMVVM1) which is responsible for carrying out the irrigation development, land
reallocation and agrarian reform of the Lower Medjerda Valley (90,000 ha), and
the smaller Nebhana and Lakhmes Development Authorities. Further development
agencies are planned for Badrouna and Mogods. The State Land Agency (Office
des Terres Domaniales - OTD) controls the approximately 750,000 ha of Govern-
ment lands (see para 16).
9.        Several semi-autonomous entities are import related:  the Coopera-
tive Centrale des Semences et de Plantes Selectionnees provides most of cereal
seeds, the Societe Nationale de la Protection des Vegetaux, carries out much
of the plant protection activities, the Cooperative Centrale des Grandes Cul-
tures is responsible for fertilizer marketing and distribution and the Societe
Nationale de Mlotoculture (SONAMO), formerly Cooperative Centrale de tIotoculture
(COCEIIO) provides small and medium farmers with tractor and harvester related
services.
10.       Agronomic Research.  Agronomic Research is concentrated around 3
main institutions: Institut National de la Recherche Agronomique Tunisienne
(Tunisian National Agronomic Research Institute - INRAT) with central labora-
tories in Tunis and 16 experimental farms spread through the country, Institut
National Agronomique de Tunis (Tunis National Agronomic Institute - INAT) and
Centre de Recherche du Genie Rural (Rural Engineering Research Center).
Regional research centers are in the Beja, Bizerte, Jendouba, Sousse and Gabes
Governorates.  According to Government claims, significant results were ob-
tained between 1970 and 1974 in the field of new varieties of high-yielding
wheat, in improvement of cultivation systems (crop rotation and weeding), in
selection of fodder plants suited to each region and in development of new
varieties of vegetables and fruit trees.
11.       Agricultural Extension Services.  The Ministry of Agriculture's
Extension Division in the Department of Agricultural and Livestock Production
is the principal agriculture extension organization in the country. However,
several other agencies and institutions also have extension responsibilities,
namely the Ministry's Crop Protection Division, Agricultural Production
Division, Livestock Production Division, Office des Terres Domaniales (OTD),
Office de Mise en Valeur de la Vallee de la Medjerda (OMVVM), Office de IMise
en Valeur Agricole de lNebhana (OMIVAN), Office National des Cereales (ONC)
(grain production) and Office National de l'Elevage et des Paturages (ONEP)
(stockraising and pastures).
12.       In 1974 the total staff serving the agriculture extension sector
was as follows:



AN4NEX I
Page 4

Services      Services      "Groupements"      Total
Chief Engineers and
Main Engineers                137              5             31              173

Assistant Engineers              24             87             118             239
Technical Assistants            300            294            464            1,058
Technical Officers              462          1,130            230            1,822
Total                       1,117          1,619            933           3,679
Of these, the Ministry's Extension Division employed 19 engineers and 6 tech-
nical officers in its head office and 35 engineers and 264 technical officers
in the field. All sectors of agriculture are said to be covered but the num-
ber of farms per extension officer is high since it is estimated that one
field officer has to supervize on average 1,200 farmers. In spite of this
the extension activities of the different agencies sometimes overlap at the
level of their contacts with farmers, with as a result conflicting advice
given to them.
13.       Agricultural Education in Tunisia is entirely under the responsi-
bility of the Ministry of Agriculture and is provided at four levels: uni-
versity, para-university, secondary and vocational. At university level,
the four-year course at the Institut National Agronomique de Tunis (INAT)
leads to the qualification of Agricultural Engineer. Para-university educa-
tion is provided by four specialized institutes (irrigation, rural engineering,
horticulture, forestry), with two-year courses leading to the qualification of
Technical Assistant. Secondary education is given by nine agricultural high
schools through four-year courses leading to middle-rank Technical Officer
positions. Vocational training which is given to adults already working is
provided at 16 centers and consists of two to four-year courses depending on
the type of specialization.
14.       The results of the various agriculture education programs appear to
be satisfactory, and there are presently in Tunisia (private, semi-public and
public sector): 700 agriculture engineers, 4,600 technicians and 2,700 per-
sons with vocational training. The following table shows the attendance of
the different forms of training in 1971/1974:

The total number has almost tripled over five years, with the increases parti-
cularly evident in respect of the vocational training centers and fishery
training. However permanent vocational training will become increasingly
urgent in order to update specialized knowledge.
15.       Land Tenure.  Basically three categories may be distinguished:
-    the state-held land, mainly rainfed farms that were taken
over from their former foreign owners. These lands were
the first to be organized as cooperatives in the late 1960s;
-    the privately owned farms of the "traditional" sector, where
land tenure problems are hampering the development of the
country's most important agricultural resources; and
-    the collectively owned lands, especially in Central and Southern
Tunisia, where population pressure has rendered the traditional
tenure system obsolete.
16.        State-Owned Lands.  Over the period 1956-64 the State acquired more
than one million ha by the purchase of foreign-owned lands and by the nationa-
lization of "habous" lands. In 1961 the "Office des Terres Domaniales" (OTD)
was established to manage these lands and preside over their final distribu-
tion. These lands have become the core of the cooperative farm structure
which at the time was intended eventually to include all private farms. After
the repeal, in September 1969, of the decrees to put under cooperative owner-
ship the entire agricultural sector, lands previously privately held were
returned to private ownership and an inventory and a review of the future role
of the remaining lands led to a plan whereby some 126,000 ha would become
state farms; 351,000 ha would remain as production cooperatives; 76,000 ha
would be allocated to demonstration, research and teaching purposes, and the
remaining, 262,000  ha would be sold to the private sector.  State farms



ANNEX 1
Page 6
are on some of the best soils and have a good production potential. The re-
maining cooperative farms are managed in the same way as state farms and con-
sequently are cooperatives only in name. It is not clear what their future
will be. The Government has stated its intention of gradually transferring
decision-making power to the members of the cooperatives, who at present are
little more than wage laborers. It seems probable that the cooperative farms
will remain de facto state farms for several years to come. Since 1970, ex-
perience has shown that the main problem with the state farms and cooperatives
is management. OTD still feels that it must exercise a high degree of central-
ized control over its farms, thus leaving little freedom to the manager.
17.       The land earmarked for transfer to the private sector is admittedly
inferior, and the main reason for its proposed transfer is that its low pro-
ductivity hampers the efforts of the state farms to become economically viable.
This land is to be disposed of in the following way:
(a) by exchange for private land in public irrigation
perimeters whose present owners prefer not to shift
to irrigated agriculture;
(b) by allocation through settlement schemes;
(c)  by direct sale of entire farms to farmers grouped
in a corporation or production cooperative; or
(d) by leasing to individuals pending final disposition.
Privately Farmed Lands
18.       Private holdings are by far the most important category of ownership.
Private farms occupy 4.5 million ha, as against 0.8 million ha of agricultural
land held by the state. Even after the return, in 1969, to the doctrine of
the coexistence of state, cooperative and private farms, a disproportionately
high share of the Government's attention in terms of investment and technical
services is still concentrated on the state and cooperative farms, even though
most of the potential for agricultural growth lies in the private farms, es-
pecially in the "traditional" sector. How much of this potential will be
realized depends in large measure on the resolution of two land tenure prob-
lems: (a) the absence of secure and registered land titles, and (2) the un-
restricted fragmentation of holdings with its corollary of concentration of
ownership.
19.       Of the total productive area (agricultural, grazing and forest) of
about 9 million ha, roughly 3 million ha have registered titles. The Land
Registry Act of 1885, providing both for a complete cadastral survey and de-
livery of a clear title and for a somewhat less secure certificate of regis-
tration, led to the registration of some 2.5 million ha, virtually all in
large ownership units in the North. This system of land registration was



ANNEX 1
Page 7
voluntary and served in the first place the interest of the European settlers
although in practice many Tunisian landowners made use of it. This system,
which was somewhat simplified in 1965, is still available, but very little
used because most of the large landowners who can afford its expenses have
already taken advantage of it. For the benefit of the smaller landowners,
a compulsory registration of land not previously registered was established
in 1964. The cost is D 50 per ha, entirely borne by the State. Of the
previously owned lands some 2 million ha have so far been titled leaving 2.5
million ha still unregistered. The time required to complete the coverage is
estimated at at least 20 years.
The Collectively-Owned Lands
20.        Some 2.1 million ha out of 9 million ha of productive land is owned
collectively by tribal groups, called "farqa", mostly in the Center and
South. 1/ These are the remmants of a tenure system that in earlier centu-
ries covered the entire country. Even now, the collective lands, which con-
sist entirely of extensive grazing lands, are continuously decreasing because
of pressure to use the more suitable areas for cereals under individual tenure.
The collective lands were in ecological equilibrium until increasing popula-
tion led to overgrazing and a disorderly increase in cereals production, with
consequent deterioration of soil and vegetative resources and gradual exten-
sion of the desert into certain areas.
21.        In 1971 the Government passed legislation intended to provide a
definitive solution to the uncertainty surrounding the future of the col-
lectively-owned lands. With the help of local boards ("Conseils de Gestion")
it aims at transforming from collective to individual ownership those lands
that are: (1) planted in tree crops either by the individuals concerned or
by the State, capital recovery charge being levied in the latter case, (2)
suitable irrigation perimeters. The total area that could thus be transformed
into private holdings has been estimated at I million ha. The remaining 1.1
million ha is by implication only suitable for grazing and would remain under
collective ownership and management.
1/   The total area of collectively-owned lands including non-productive land
is about 6 million ha.

A. Date Palm Plantation
1.        Tunisia's date production dates since early times and is concen-
trated in the semi-desertic Southern and Southwestern part of the country.
The bulk of the date production comes still from traditional oases which cover
some 10,500 ha. The production of the last 5 years varied, according to
climatic conditions and the availability of groundwater, between 39,000 and
58,000 tons of which the praised Deglat variety represented about 20%. Date
exports - nearly exclusively the Deglat variety amounted during the same
period on average to about 5,800 tons per year.
2.        The best suited areas in Tunisia for high quality date production
are the Nefzaoua and Djerid areas situated respectively South and North of
Chott El Djerid. It is also in these regions that STIL owns presently over
1,000 ha of modern date plantations, the greater part of which were financed
under the IDA/Bank First Agricultural Credit Project.
3.        Traditional oases cultivation in the Djerid accounts for a total
area of around 3,400 ha. Characteristic of these traditional oases are the
high density of trees (between 250 and 300 trees per hectare), outdated
methods of cultivation (largely due to lack of resources) and mediocre yields.
The system of land tenure is marked by an extreme fragmentation of ownership,
so that in some cases the fruit of a single palm tree may belong to more than
one family. Larger holdings are most often managed indirectly in one of the
.ollowing forms:
-    the "khammessat", based on a division of the harvest
between the landowner (4/5) and the tenant (1/5);
-    the letting of land against payment of a rental;
-    the "norgharsa", a tree-planting lease (fruit trees).
4.        In these oases the ownership of water is generally separate from
that of land. For centuries irrigation has been practiced with spring water
whose flow rates were originally very substantial (in 1920 1,930 I/sec in
the Djerid). With the introduction of well-drilling techniques, numerous
tubewells have been sunk, initially close to springs, with a view of increas-
ing the volume of water available for traditional date palm plantations and
subsequently also for the development of modern oasis cultivation. By March
1974, 33 artesian wells and 18 pump wells had been completed in the Djerid.
Slowly the volume of flow has started to decrease with a corresponding con-
tinuous fall in the watertable: by 1973, of a total discharge of 2,045 1/sec
the natural springs represented only 1,030 1/sec i.e. 50% of the total, where-
as artesian tubewells accounted for 32% (650 l/sec) and pump wells for 18%
(365 1/sec).



AINNEX I
Appendix I
Page 2
The Government has begun to rehabilitate the old palm groves:  at
Nefta (DJerid) 865 hectares have been provided with an irrigation system of
semicircular flumes and a ntmber of additional wells have been drilled; at
11 Ouidane (Degache) 721 hectares have been provided with a main 9 km pipeline
connecting several wells, compensating reservoirs and secondary pipelines;
inprovements to the tertiary system have yet to be made. The Government's
investment in the rehabilitation works amounts already to D 1.2 million;
the total cost of the scheme covering 1,200 ha is estimated at n 3 million.
6.        New Plantations comprise:
(i)  Young oases with a maximum age of about 14 years; these in-
clude modern privately owned plantations (570 hectares),
some of which are managed by STIL and date palm plantations
developed by STIL between 1966 and 1970 covering an area of
282 hectares in the Djerid;
(ii) In 1971, under the Bank's First Agricultural Credit Project,
STIL was to plant about 900 ha of new date palm groves in
Djerid and Nefzaoua. As of November 1975, 550 ha were suc-
cessfully developed and planted in the Djerid and 263 in
Nefzaoua; plantation of a further 73 ha is scheduled for the
current season. Some of the local population, impressed by
the apparent success of the STIL venture, have formed farmer
development associations (Societes Civiles de Mlise en Valeur -
see Para 14 below) and are extremely keen to continue planting
on their own. Among the 14 SCMIVs approved and registered so
far, three have started development work. Their plantations
will represent a total of some 100 ha when completed and are
all located near Nefta in the Djerid area.
7.        Yields and Production.  The date palm commences to produce dates
from the third or fourth year after planting, but quantity is small and the
quality of the dates is mediocre. By the 7th year production reaches about
20 kg per tree, increasing each year by about 10 kg/tree, and from the 15th
year to the age of 60 years the date palm is in full production yielding, in
a modern plantation, between 80 and 120 kgs. After the 60th year the yield
starts to decrease and the replacement of the palms has to be envisaged.
Average yields in Tunisia in traditional groves are 30 kg/tree for the
Deglats and 40 kg/tree for ordinary varieties.
8.        Tunisian production averaged between 1971 and 1974 45,000 tons of
which 12,000 tons were Deglats; exports amounted over the same period in
average to some 6,300 tons a year, of which 5,800 tons were Deglats; the
balance, representing some 85% of all dates produced, is consumed locally.
9.       Marketing.  The date has been and still is the staple food of the
oases and Saharan peoples. With the growth of urban consumption centers in



ANNEX 1
Appendix 1
Page 3
Tunisia and with the expansion of export markets, the ordinary varieties of
dates traditionally cultivated no longer meet the market requirements. The
Deglat has become the most sought after variety; its commercial value has
risen rapidly from D 100- 130/ton in 1970 to D 200 - 250/ton in 1974 and
D 280/ton in 1975. (The officially established floor prices - actual prices
are frequently higher).
10.       Producers frequently sell their dates as a standing crop to the
khammessat, who then assume responsibility for harvesting, sorting, crating
and resale to wholesalers. Wholesalers may also purchase standing crops
direct from producers. They then provide transportation at their own expense
and deliver the dates either to merchants in Tunis or Sfax or to exporters.
Up to 1964/65 dates were exported by Tunisian merchants who sold their product
to firms in Marseilles. Initially these firms established a whole series
of industrial facilities for the processing of imported dates; subsequently
they formed a single grouping (Europe Dattes). To protect the Tunisian pro-
duction and to increase exports, Government granted STIL a monopoly for the
marketing and export of dates. In 1972 Government abolished STIL's monopoly
and since 1973 a second company formed at Soliman (Cap Bon) also has authority
to export.
11.       Prices of Deglat dates on export markets have constantly risen; they
are high in relation to prices for Iraki and Algerian dates mainly because
they are better processed and presented. As a result Tunisia is selling its
date production on foreign markets at a higher price and quicker. France,
which is the major importer, also re-exports part of its purchases to Italy,
and to various other European countries (particularly Germany).
12.       Development Prospects.  The essential factor to further development
of date palm plantation is the availability of irrigation water. A regional
study undertaken under UNESCO (1968-1971) concluded that the problem was less
one of resources than of methods and operating costs. A recently concluded
water resource study of the Tunisian South, prepared by the French consulting
firm SOGREAH concluded that the Djerid irrigated area could - as far as
potential water resources are concerned, be increased from the present 4,750
ha to about 10,750 ha. The data are similar for the Nefzaoua area (Annex 1,
Appendix 1, Table 1).
13.       Drilling of new wells has however contributed to a drop in the
groundwater level, and consequent decrease of yields of the existing wells in
the old oases. A program for the expansion of new palm groves has to be
accompanied by a rehabilitation of the old oases with additional pumps and
equipment needed to maintain current rates of flow. Such a program, start of
which has already been made at Nefta and Degache, is projected by the Mfinistry
of Agriculture as part of the Master Plan for Development of Southern Water
Resources to be based on the results of the above mentioned study by SOGREAH.

Appendix I
Page 4
B. Societes Civiles de Mise en Valeur
14.       The Societes Civiles de Mise en Valeur (SCMVs) were created under
a 1971 law to allow for joint purchase and cultivation of land by groups of
individual farmers. They are, in fact, but not in name, small cooperatives.
Each member owns shares for an amount equivalent to his contribution (in
cash or in kind) to the SCMV. The members are jointly and severally liable.
15.       The SCMVs were created principally with a view to allow farmers in
the Southern oases to jointly dig and use a deep well for date palm planta-
tions. The State sells the land to the SCMV for a symbolic amount, but with
certain obligations (to dig a well, to plant palm trees). Out of 40 applica-
tions to create an SCMV, 15 have been approved and 3 already have completed
their wells and started planting.
16.       The first SCMVs had few members, about a dozen, usually richer farm-
ers. Those most recently created have about 50 members, usually smaller
farmers. All the SCMVs created so far are in the Djerid and cover about 50 ha
each.
17.       According to the law, the SCMVs are created for at least thirty
years and cannot be dissolved earlier, so as to provide for joint cultiva-
tion of the land. Actually most SCMVs seem to be inclined not to continue
to cultivate the land in a cooperative form but, once the investment is com-
pleted and loans are fully repaid, leave the cultivation of the individual
plots to the SCMV member.
18.       The present SCMV subprojects evaluation procedures to be applied for
plantations financed from Government FOSDA funds are as follows:
(a) Grant of land to SCMV by Government Land Administration (Office
des Terres Domaniales OTD);
(b) Technical study by the Government water resources administration
(Direction des Ressources en Eaux Souterraines, DRES);
(c) Approval by the Ministry of Agriculture (Direction de la Produc-
tion Agricole, DPA);
(d) Decision by BNT defining the amount of loan, subborrowers con-
tribution, disbursement modalities and other terms of loan;
(e) Establishment of joint and several liability documents binding
all members of the SCMV, and of mortgage of SCMV's land as
soon as Title is received.
(For representative operational model of a 75 ha date plantation, see Annex 5
page 3 and Tables 13 and 14).

Olive Oil Production
1.        Olive growing and olive oil production is one of Tunisia's oldest
agricultural activities and dates back to the Ronan colonisation. Some 1.25
million ha are presently planted with over 50 million olive trees. Olive
production has two outstanding features, namely (a) olive trees are among the
few crops that produce under semi-arid conditions (average rainfall as low
as 150-200 mm) and can survive successive years of severe drought, and (b)
the olive production is notorious for its fluctuations from year to year due
partly to rainfall but also to other factors which are not well understood.
The Tunisian olive harvest between 1969/70 and 1974/75 varied between 125,000
tons and 901,000 tons and olive oil exports represented between 1971 and 1974
in value on average about 50% of Tunisia's agriculture exports.
2.        Although olive production is practiced throughout Tunisia North of
MIedenine, it is heavily concentrated in 3 zones: Sfax, the Sahel, and the
Mejerda Valley. In Sfax it is the predominant crop and 80% of the culti-
vable land is planted with olive trees. Cultivation practices vary according
to rainfall: in the North, tree density is normally 100-130 trees/ha, often
intercropped with vineyards or annual crops. Towards the south tree density
declines and, in the Sfax region, tree density is as low as 20 trees/ha,
without intercropping. In all regions, olive trees are often mixed with
other fruit trees, such as almond or pistachio trees. With the current age
structure of trees, the average oil yield/ha is 220 kg in the tiejerda Val-
ley, 200 kg in the Sahel and 130 in Sfax. Under the various development
plans of the 1960's an important planting progran was carried out, of which
the first effects will be felt during the second half of the 1970's; during
the 1980's these trees will come into full production.
3.        A survey of land utilization in 1971 and 1972 estimated the number
of olive trees at about 52 million, occupying an acreage of some 1,242,000 ha,
of these:
(a) 21% were classified as "very young", i.e. less than
10 years;
(h) 27% were between 10 and 20 years and in early production;
(c) 45% were adult trees, in full production;
(d)  7% were old, over 70 years, with declining yields.
As more of the younger trees come into production or into full production,
only partially offset by the falling yields of the older trees, average
olive production can be expected to increase by some 6.5% p.a. between now
and 1985.

5.        An inventory of existing grinding capacity in each governorate made
in 1973 by the National Edible Oil Board (ONH) shows that the required capacity
for 1981 compared to the present one (1975) will be:

6.        According to the inventory by governorates, the deficit will be
greatest in the Tunis, Beja, Jendouba and Siliana governorates in the North,
and in Kasserine in the central region; there will be surplus capacity in
the South due to the ageing of the olive groves. To meet the expected regional
shortage of olive oil extraction capacity in Northern and Central Tunisia,
about one hundred new mills (30 T/day each) have to be built by 1981.

APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
TUNISIA
Agricultural Credit
A. Financial Environment
Introduction
1.        The Tunisian Banking System comprises the Central Bank (Banque
Centrale de Tunisie, BCT); 12 commercial banks (of which 3 are foreign-
owned and 1 in the process of liquidation); 3 investment banks (Banque de
Developpement Economique de Tunisie, BDET), Compagnie Financiere et Tour-
istique COFITOUR, Banque Generale d'Investissement, BGI); 1 development fi-
nance company (Societe Financiere et de Gestion, SOFIGES); 43 local mutual
credit banks (Caisses Locales de Credit Mutuel; CLCM); the Postal Checking
Accounts System; the Housing Savings Fund and the National Savings Bank.
There are also several insurance companies and a Stock Exchange (Bourse). In
addition, the Treasury receives deposits and performs some banking operations
for government agencies and public and semi-public enterprises. Three
government-owned institutions have played a central role in the Government's
credit policy and investment program: two commercial banks, Societe
Tunisienne de Banque (STB) and Banque Nationale de Tunisie (BNT) and an in-
surance company, Societe Tunisienne d'Assurances et de Reassurances (STAR).
The Banking Law
2.        The Banking Law 67-51 of December 7, 1967, modified in January,
1975, sets forth the conditions governing the operations of banking insti-
tutions. Each bank has to be authorized by the Central Bank and must specify
whether it intends to work as a commercial bank or as an investment bank.
The commercial banks are entitled to receive deposits and they may invest
them either in short-term loans or, within certain limits set by BCT, in
medium-term loans (not more than seven years); they may also grant long-
term loans out of special funds. The investment banks may receive only fixed
term deposits for more than one year; they finance investments through equity
participation and medium- and long-term loans. The law and subsequent BCT
regulations spell out certain requirements (see below paras 4-6: The Control
of Credit).
The Central Bank
3.        The Central Bank, in addition to its functions as currency-issuing
bank and custodian of foreign reserves, acts as banker to the Government and



ANNEX 2
Page 2
to the commercial banks. It accepts deposits from the banks and makes
payments on their behalf; it may discount Treasury bills and Government-
guaranteed securities or accept them as collateral. It may rediscount com-
mercial paper, loans for crop marketing (cereals, wine and olive oil), short-
term and medium-term loans. In order to control the overall volume of credit
extended by the commercial banks, BCT relies primarily on the following:
(a) Rediscount rate and control of the banks' rate structure;
(b) Prior approval of bank loans above specified amounts;
(c) Variations in the rediscount ceilings;
(d) Ratios affecting the operations of the commercial banks;
(e) Reserve requirements;
(f) Moral persuasion, instructions to banks and penalties.
The Control of Credit
4.         The main provisions of the Banking Law in respect to banks are
as follows:
(a) Mlinimum paid-in capital of D 200,000.
(b) Minimum allocation to legal reserves of 20% of the net yearly
profit until equity reaches a certain percentage of deposits
set by BCT ("solvency ratio" of 10%).
(c) Fixed assets shall not exceed 75% of equity.
(d) Participation in the equity capital of any given company
shall not exceed 10% of equity and reserves of the bank, nor
shall it exceed 30% of the capital of the company (this provi-
sion has not been strictly enforced).
(e) Banks must close their accounts on each December 31; the ac-
counts must be certified by a "Commissaire aux Comptes" (ex-
ternal comptroller); verification of accounts is usually
performed only from an arithmetical point of view and by
checks against supporting documents.
5.        The regulations through which BCT can control credit are:
(a) The Rediscount Rate: The basic rate has been 5% since 1966. In
certain sectors BCT applies differential rates: export credit,
seasonal crop financing and medium-term credit for housing



ANNEX 2
Page 3
guaranteed by the Government (4%); medium-term credit for
large industrial facilities, for agricultural equipment,
for tourism investments, for investments in the poorer re-
gions and for investments by export-oriented companies is
rediscounted at 4.25%.
(b) Control of the Banks' Rate Structure
(i)  Deposits:  Banks are allowed to pay interest on deposits
at rates ranging from 0.5 to 6.5% (see Table 1).
(ii) Lending Rates: Interest rates charged by banks on their
lending operations are also subject to BCT control, which
sets minimum and maximum rates for certain types of trans-
actions, depending if the loan is rediscountable or not.
Rates vary from 4.75%O (export financing) to 8% (non-
rediscountable medium-term loans). These rates do not
apply to loans made from budgetary resources or foreign
borrowings (see Table 1).
(c) Prior Approval of Bank Loans
(i)  Short-term loans above D 5,000 are rediscountable only
with BCT approval and rediscount of only a portion of
the loan may be granted (except for agricultural and
olive oil seasonal credit, where the limit is D 20,000
and for commercial paper discount, where the limit is
D 150,000). Furthermore, loans above D 150,000 for
commercial transactions, government-financed transac-
tions, agricultural, mining and some industrial acti-
vities, and all loans above D 50,000 in other sectors
cannot be made without prior BCT approval.
(ii) Medium-term loans always require prior BCT authorization
(except for new investment projects of new companies
agreed by the Agence de Promotion des Investissements
(API) or the Tourism Agreement Committee) and are re-
discountable only with BCT approval.
(d) Rediscount Ceilings
(i)  Regular ceiling of D 12 million is subdivided into
ceilings for each bank; since January, 1974, no financ-
ing above the regular ceiling is allowed but banks are
authorized to borrow on the interbank money market in-
stead.



ANNEX 2
Page 4
(ii) Seasonal ceilings for financing of the marketing of grain
(D 11.5 million), wine (D 2.6 million) and olive oil
(D 15.5 million). Use of the rediscount ceilings is
shown in Table 1. Their use has increased in 1975 to
about 70-80%, mostly because of the heavy demand for
olive oil financing.
(iii) Medium-term ceiling of D 12 million, to which a bank
may have access only if it has invested at least 15%
of its deposits in medium-term loans (use of resources
ratio). The medium-term ceiling is used at 51-57%.
(e) Ratios: The five ratios used by BCT are shown in Table 2.
(f) Reserve Requirements: Banks have to deposit with BCT 6.5%
of their own deposits except for non-residents' blocked accounts
where the requirement is 40% (in September 1975, reserves with
BCT amounted to D 27.2 million).
6.        These control measures are intended to contribute to the four gen-
eral objectives of the money and credit policy of Tunisia:
(a) the control of banking liquidity;
(b) the incentive system to finance investments;
(c) the financing of seasonal activity;
(d) the encouragement of savings.
B. Demand and Supply of Agricultural Credit
Regulation of Agricultural Credit
7.        The Agricultural Planning and Development Office in the Ministry of
Agriculture is in charge of the general planning of the agricultural sector
and of the global evaluation of the credit needs of agriculture. The Consulta-
tive Committee on Agricultural Credit and Incentives, comprising representa-
tives of BNT and the Ministry of Agriculture, is advising the Government on
credit policies and recommending the allocation of budgetary resources for
agricultural credit. It also decides on the granting of loans by the Fonds
Special pour le Developpement Agricole (FOSDA); however, because of the great
number of FOSDA loans it has to review (about 10,000 loan applications, some-
times more than 1,000 per meeting), it often merely endorses recommendations
made by the staff of the Ministry and of BNT without discussing the individual
investment projects. BCT, in addition to its general regulation activities,
is directly involved in rediscounting short-term credit for olive oil, wine
and cereals; it also refinances stocks of citrus for exports outside of the



ANNEX 2
Page 5
normal rediscount ceiling. The olive oil rediscount facility has been over-
drawn because of export marketing difficulties.
Short-term Credit to Agriculture
8.        No surveys have been made on non-institutional credit by merchants
and big landowners and no data is available. Short-term credit to agricul-
ture in cash is provided mainly by BNT and the CLCMs, although a few commer-
cial farmers are clients of other commercial banks (less than 10% of the total
amount). The amount of short-term credit extended by the banking system to
the agricultural sector and its sources is shown in Table 3. BNT and CLCM
short-term credit is described in more detail in Annex 3.
9.        However, many farmers do not have access to credit in cash from
banks, but receive credit in kind from various government agencies, which
either are specialized by crop and type of farming (grain, oil, livestock)
or manage state farms (Office des Terres Domaniales; OTD) or specific re-
gions (Office de Mise en Valeur de la Vallee de la Medjerda; OMVVM) or pro-
vide mechanization services (SONAMO). The terms of these loans are often a
cash payment upon delivery of the inputs (seeds, fertilizers, plant protec-
tion materials, or mechanization services) and payment of the remainder at
the time of harvest; they are often not considered loans, and not accounted
as such. The various agencies responsible for this type of credit are
described in Annex 1. Data about the volume of loans extended is unreliable
and unprecise. Normally, there is no interest charge.
(a) The Grain Agency (Office National des Cereales; ONC) extends
credit for seeds, fertilizers, and herbicides to about 4,000
farmers, most of them large-size commercial farmers. In 1972
/73 it provided D 485,000 of inputs, and in 1973/74 D 611,000.
Loans are for 12 months at 3%, and borrowers' contribution
(initial cash payment) is 25%; repayments are said to be good,
since the harvest is mortgaged and sold to ONC.
(b) Several grain cooperatives (Cooperative Centrale de Ble,
COCEBLE; Cooperative Centrale de Semences, COSem) Coope-
rative Centrale des Grandes Cultures, CCGC) supervised by
ONC have similar programs reaching at most 5,000 large-size
farmers (about D 500,000).
(c) The National Edible Oil Agency (Office National de l'Huile;
ONH) and the Livestock and Pasture Agency (Office National
de l'Elevage et des Paturages; ONEP) are more active in
medium-term credit (see paras 13 and 18) but also provide
some short-term credit (D 200,000).
(d) The Medjerda Valley Development Agency (OMVVM) provides all
inputs under credit to farmers in its area together with
technical assistance. Borrowers' contribution is 10% and
the total amount of loans for 1971/72 was D 350,000; for



ANNEX 2
Page 6
1972/73, D 492,000; and for 1973174, D 600,000 (about 5,000
farmers). Under the recent Irrigation Rehabilitation Project
(1068-TUN) credit may be provided to about 4,000 small farm-
ers through OMVVM and through a similar agency in the Nebhana
region (OMIVAN).
(e)  The State Land Agency (OTD) provides very little credit itself
(D 19,000 in 1973/74) but cooperates with SONAMO, previously
the Central Mechanization Cooperative (COCEMO), in the provision
of loans in kind for mechanization services and some inputs.
SONANfO reaches about 11,000 farmers directly and 8,000 through
the mechanization services distribution centers; in collaboration
with OTD.
All these credit programs in kind can be estimated to reach at the very most
40,000 farmers, for an amount of at most D 4 million.
10.       70% of the short-term credit is not given as part of an extension
and input package but disbursed in cash by BNT and the CLCM. The sectoral
distribution of short-term credit does not seem to be in line with the
national priorities or the contribution of each crop to total production,
but it is better explained by banking criteria. Grain and vegetable culti-
vation, which account for 35% of total production, received about 75% of
BNT and CLCMI credit. The tentative distribution for 1972/73 was:

Grain farmers borrowing from BNT are usually commercial farmers and BNT is
channelling most of its short-term resources to them.  The large amount of
CLCM credit for the cultivation of vegetables is due to the importance of the
CLCMs in the Cap Bon region. Compared to BNT, however, the CLCM loans are
distributed in a fashion somewhat closer to the structure of production in
the region where they are provided.
11.       The total number of farmers having access to short-term credit, in
cash or in kind, is probably no more than 50,000 or 15% of the total number of
private farmers in the country (only 3% of the total receive credit in cash).
While credit in kind may reach farmers in various categories, credit by BNT



ANNEX 2
Page 7
(and the other commercial banks) reaches large farmers who can borrow about
D 1,600, and the CLCM reach smaller farmers, with average loans of D 275. Al-
though data are very limited, it has been estimated (on the basis of regional
surveys) that about 50% of commercial farmers (e.g., more than 50 ha in the
Jendouba Governorate) have access to BNT short-term credit, 30% of medium-size
farmers (10 to 50 ha) receive credit either from CLCM or in kind, and 5% of
small farmers (less than 10 ha) receive credit in kind.
12.       The regional breakdown of short-term credit also shows important dis-
parities (Table 3). The North receives 90% of the short-term credit. This is
linked to the disparities in financing specific activities; for example, live-
stock, whose share of financing is less than its share of total agricultural
production, is one of the main activities in the Center and South, while grain,
whose share of financing is more than its share of production, is grown mostly
in the North. The CLCtls provide most of the short-term credit in the Cap Bon
(citrus and vegetables) and in the Sahel (olives).
Long-term Credit to Agriculture
13.       This encompasses also medium-term credit (between 1 and 7 years).
The amount of long-term credit outstanding from the banking system to the
agricultural sector is shown in Table 4. From 1961-71 the average sources of
investment funds for agriculture have been: state 52.5%, foreign aid 18.5%,
loans disbursed through the banks 7%, loans disbursed through state agencies
0.8%, grants disbursed through the banks 1.2% and self-financing 20%. Most
long-term credit is channelled through BNT, but only a negligible amount is
financed by BNT's own resources: most of the loans are financed from budget-
ary sources and foreign aid. Long-term credit outside the banking system is
limited to the financing of livestock (sheep and heifers) by ONEP (D 500,000/
year). BNT lending is described in more detail in Annex 3. Some of the in-
vestment loans financed from budgetary resources are part of a financing
package that provides also a grant to cover part of the investment costs;
this grant is also disbursed by BNT.
14.       The volume of BNT long-term lending is shown in Table 4.  Less than
3% of long-term credit comes from BNT's own funds. Such a situation is in
direct contrast with the short-term credit situation. BNT, who has to use
14% of its deposits in medium-term loans, prefers to invest them in industry
and services, with safer and more profitable investments and higher interest
rates, and has so far not been encouraged to do otherwise.
15.       In the case of long-term credit, the correlation between the import-
ance of an activity and the amount of credit received is somewhat stronger
than in the case of short-term credit:

The grain sector again seems to receive a bigger share of investment and
loans, but it should be noted that the agricultural machinery is also prob-

very little investment and credit for a variety of reasons (poorer farmers,
low milk price acting as a disincentive to invest). The heavy share of
investment under land improvement is mostly financed by the state (dams,
irrigation system) or by credit under other categories.
16.       The total number of long-term loans to private farmers through
BNT was 19,500 in 1973, 28,400 in 1974 but only 14,000 in 1975. Possibly not
more than 1,000 farmers receive credit from ONEP for livestock. At most 10%
of all private farmers receive long-term credit. The group reached by aach
fund will be discussed in Annex 3, but beneficiaries range from small farmers
under the World Food Program (WFP) in the Center and South, to medium-size
farmers under FOSDA to large commercial farmers under IBRD/IDA.
17.       The regional breakdown of long-term credit shows a predominance
of the North (half of total) but less disparities than for short-term
credit, probably because of the influence of WFP (and to some extent
FOSDA) in the regional diversification (financing of olive trees, date
palm trees, and to a lesser extent livestock). (See Table 4).

C. Credit to Agro-Industries
18.       Total credit to agroindustries is shown in Table 5.  Credit from
outside the banking system is provided only by the National Edible Oil Agency
(ONH) which gives short-term and (interest free) medium-term credit for ex-
tension and rehabilitation of olive oil extraction plants. These medium-term
credits are for rehabilitation and modernization of existing plants and their
storage facilities (but not for creation of new facilities); they amount to
about D 50,000 to D 100,000 per year, but their volume is likely to increase
in view of the present difficulties in oil marketing and resulting shortage
of storage facilities. 1/
19.       Short-term credit to agroindustries includes seasonal rediscount-
able credit for financing stocks of olive oil to ONH, of wine to the Union
Centrale des Cooperatives de Vinification (UCCV) and of grain to ONC, COCEBLE,
CCGC and COSem; financing of grain stocks is provided only by BNT, the other
two by a banking pool. These credits are partially rediscountable under BCT
rediscount ceilings, up to 80% for wine, 75% for grain and 60% for oil (prob-
ably to be increased to 80%). The interest rates are 5.75% on the rediscount-
able portion and 6.75% on the remainder, thus leading to average rates of
5.95% for wine, 6% for grain and 6.15% for oil (to decrease to 5.95%). Re-
payment is made when the stocks are sold, normally within a year, but with
longer delays now in the case of oil. The use of these rediscount facilities
is given in Table 1. Similar credits are also given in relatively small
amounts for fruits and vegetables (to private traders) and for dates to the
Societe Tunisienne de l'Industrie Laitiere (STIL).
20.       BDET loans to agroindustries have been as follows (commitments):

21.       The banking sector has also taken equity participations in agro-
industrial companies. Although precise data is not available, it can be
estimated that 7% of the BDET portfolio (about D 300,000), 22% of the BNT
portfolio (D 250,000) and a very small proportion of the STB (and SOFIGES)
portfolio (D 150,0000), or a total of about D 700,000, is invested by banks
in agroindustry, of which almost half in the sugar mill company, Societe
Tunisienne du Sucre (STS), and 20% in the STIL group. BNT's portfolio is
detailed in Annex 3.
D. Agricultural Credit Policy
22.       As can be seen from the above description, agricultural credit in
Tunisia is characterized by the great number of institutions involved and by
the diversity of financing terms under various funds. This situation is the
result of the piecemeal approach of the Tunisian Government, which has not
elaborated a global agricultural credit policy encompassing all institutions
and all funds involved, but instead has met every challenge as it came,
creating new institutions or new funds or letting institutions enter the
credit field to compensate for BNT's inability or reluctance to do so. This
framework, while allowing a certain flexibility of credit provisions which
could be adapted to target groups, has had detrimental effects both in
institutional and economic terms, which were compounded by the easy lending
terms and conditions (low interest rates, high grant element).
23.       In institutional terms, not only is the control of credit at the
national level made difficult by the multiplicity of credit-giving institu-
tions outside the banking system (thus leading possibly to an excessive
credit demand by large farmers who have access to several sources of credit,
from both banks and national agencies), but also the precise delimitation
of the roles of the various institutions involved is not always clear. A
case in point is credit to small farmers. The only institutions especially
geared to small farmers' credit needs, the CLCNIs, are left somewhat dormant,
with no attempts made to improve and reorganize them, but also with no clear
design of terminating them and integrating their clientele in the new struc-
tures. At the same time, a new mechanism, the Societes de Caution Mutuelle
(SCMs), is set up, with heavy publicity, important Government resources
(through BNT) and great expectations, but with no clear policy statement



ANNEX 2
Page 11
that they are to replace the CLCMs. The institutional scene seems to be
spLit between several institutions, each trying to maintain its own credit
activities, with little coordination with others, while the Government
places its expectations with and favors sometimes one and sometimes another.
The low interest rates on long-term agricultural lending have led the banks
to invest their funds in industry (8% or 9%) or in short-term lending to
agriculture (6%) rather than in medium- and long-term loans to agriculture.
The financing of agricultural investments had therefore to be provided by
the Government.
24.       In economic terms, the availability to farmers in some cases of
several credit sources for the same object with different conditions and at
different rates has often led to a competitive situation where the credit
demand was high for those funds with low rates, cursory project appraisal,
little supervision and low repayment pressure to the detriment of funds with
higher interest rates, thorough appraisal and supervision and high repayment
pressure (such as the first Bank loan). This situation has been only partly
corrected through administrative decisions defining the eligibility of partic-
ular groups of farmers for specific funds. Also the tendency not to press
very hard for recovery of some loans (which was much stronger earlier, but
still exists) has led many farmers to confuse loans and grants and to be
delinquent in repayments, despite heavier penalties and the (somewhat theore-
tical and not always strictly enforced) resulting inability to borrow again.
25.       The liberal credit policy has also had some detrimental effects on
the economy.  It represents a heavy burden on the budget, because of the sub-
sidized interest rates and the important grant element. For example at end
1974, D 2,073,000 (D 3.1 million in 1975) had been disbursed during the year
(through BNT) as grants under FOSDA, Fonds d'Encouragement de la Peche (FOSEP),
Fonds Allemand d' Irrigation (FAI/FAIN), and WFP; excluding Bank funds, out-
standing medium-term loans for D 27,667,500 accounted for an annual interest
subsidy of D 1,940,000 (under the assumption of a 3% average lending rate and
a 10% opportunity cost of capital). The loans in arrears which are also sup-
ported by the budget amounted to D 3,190,000 at end October 1975 of which
about D 2.5 million would have to be written off by the Government. The bud-
get has therefore to finance agriculture through credit subsidies in rather
substantial amounts on a recurring basis.
26.       The low level of interest rates also has an impact on the invest-
ment structure. As a global pattern it encourages capital-intensive invest-
ments, does not generate adequate employment opportunities in the rural areas
and therefore encourages migration to the cities, thus creating a vicious
circle of lack of manpower in the rural areas and subsequent demand for more
labor replacing, capital-intensive investments. However, the demand for
labor in the other sectors of the economy also has to be taken into account
and rural employment should not be created for employment's sake, but employ-
ment generation should be one of the objectives of agricultural policy to be
weighed against others like output, trade balance and regional development
(taking into account the impact of the level of interest rates on investment
design). The rural employment situation is discussed in Annex 1.



ANNEX 2
Page 12
27.       In social terms the credit policies have not resulted in a clear
strategy for providing credit to small farmers as a target group but have
attempted various, sometimes conflicting, limited measures whose impact has
been minimal in terms of improving the access to credit for the poorer portion
of the rural population. The limited access of small farmers to the credit
system may be partially due to lack of adequate security for loans (lack of
title to land), but the main reason is the inability or unwillingness of BNT,
the main provider of agricultural credit, to expand its lending to small farm-
ers. BNT considers itself a commercial bank and is reluctant to engage in
a small farm lending program where costs would be high. The only institutions
or projects in the past reaching small farmers were either quite ineffective
because of institutional problems (e.g., the CLCMs), or limited in scope
(WFP), being sometimes only pilot projects. Only about 15% of all Tunisian
farmers at most have access to credit. Government and BNT's management have
just begun to address the need to formulate credit policies which allow for
credit to reach small farmers in an effective manner and still be recollected.
28.       Under a covenant under the Irrigation Rehabilitation Project (1068-
TUN), the Government (Ministry of Agriculture) agreed to carry out a study
which would "encompass a critical review of the conditions presently govern-
ing agricultural credit in Tunisia taking into account Tunisian credit condi-
tions in general, agricultural income of farmers, and priorities and objec-
tives of production by crop or groups of crops," and to submit the study and
the subsequent recommendations to the Council of Ministers before October
31, 1975, so that the decision on implementation of the recommended changes
could have been taken by end November 1975. Bank staff have reviewed the
drafts of decrees containing the new set of lending policies and procedures
for FOSDA at various stages since January 1976. Government hopes to pass the
decrees in late 1976.
29.       The draft recommendations (Table 6) represent concrete progress in
two respects:
(i) An income based definition of small/medium farmers who would
be eligible for concessionary financing under FOSDA aimed at
insuring eligibility to smaller farmers; and
(ii) An increase in interest rates from 2-4.5% to 6% in all cases
except loans for deep wells and soil and water conservation
(4%) and loans for soil and water conservation, tree crops
and forage production, on which no interest would be charged
during the non-productive period.
The maximum loan amounts per ha or per head of livestock, the proportion of
investment grants and farmers' contributions and the repayment terms have
also been revised.

medium-term rediscount ceiling.
3/  If the actual ratio is below the prescribed ratio, the missing amount is blocked by BCT
(in Sept. 1975, a total of D 3.7 million was blocked).
4/  This ratio is gradually increasing from 15% on 9/30/75 to 18% on 6/30/75 by 1% each quarter.   a z
