APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
TUNISIA
A. Banque Nationale de Tunisie (BNT)
Institutional Aspects
1.        The stated primary objective of BNT is to promote agricultural de-
velopment by providing short-, medium- and long-term loans, and, more general-
lv, to carry on all normal banking and financial operations, includ-ing com-
mercial loans; it can also take equity investments. BNT operates under the
Banking Law and BCT regulations (see Annex 2).
2.        Under the First Project a policy statement was agreed as a condi-
tion of effectiveness between BNT and the Bank at the time of negotiations.
This statement, although promulgated, does not seem to have been enforced;
only some of its covenants were put into practice. The Loan Agreement, how-
ever, stated that any change in the policy statement, which would materially
Ind adversely affect the objectives, financial conditions or operations of
BNIT was an event of suspension, or of acceleration of maturity. The cove-
nants materially affecting BNT are described later in this Annex.
3.        BNT's paid-in capital increased from D 0.4 million in 1965-67 to
D 1.6 million in 1969-73 to D 3.2 million in 1974-75 and to D 4 million by
November 1975. It is distributed as follows (as of October 1975):
Tunisian Government .........      .............................  25%
Government-controlled Corporations and Cooperatives ...... 43.6%
CLCM .....................................................   16.9%
Private Shareholders ........      .............................  14.5%
A covenant in the Loan Agreement for the First Project stipulated that BNT
shall exercise its rights under subscriptions and agreements to subscribe
to its capital shares so as to ensure receipt of the capital funds available
to it promptly as needed for its operations. In this respect, BNT's sub-
scribed capital was increased from D 1.6 million to D 4 million on May 25,
1974, but was fully paid only in December 1975, i.e., more than 18 months
later.
4.        BNT is administered by a board whose members serve for six years:
it consists of a chairman (who is also the general manager), the deputy
general manager, three Government representatives, four representatives of
state companies and two private members. A financial comptroller, who is
not a 3oard member, is in charge of safeguarding the Government's interests
in Bl'TT.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
5.        BNT's head office is in Tunis; there are 3 agencies in the city and
28 in the rest of the country, all in important cities or tourist centers.
Total staff (including those on secondment to CLOts) number about 900, of
which 64% are at Headquarters, 25% in branches and 11% with the CLCMs; 30% of
the staff has a university education but few are specialized in agriculture
or agricultural economics. An important management problem encountered by
BNT has been the lack of continuity at the top management level due to the
fast turnover of presidents (having had the same president for its first ten
years, BNT then had three presidents in five years), none of them staying
long enough to outline and fully implement an overall development policy for
BNT and for agricultural credit in general.
Operating Policies and Procedures
6.        BNT is making loans to agriculture out of its own funds almost ex-
clusively for short-term credit. The resources for medium- and long-term
loans to agriculture come from several different funds, and each fund has
different rules about appraisal procedures. On FOSDA/FOSEP 1/, the technical
appraisal is done by a Ministry of Agriculture representative, and the finan-
cial appraisal (basically a creditworthiness study) by BNT. On FAI/FAIN, 2/,
SIDA 3/ and PAM 4/ loans, the decision is taken by the respective Project Man-
agers who seek BNT's advice. On FSA 5/ and Bank loans, BNT staff (actually
the Bank Project Unit) is in charge of appraisal.
7.        The provisions under the First Project for training and project im-
plementation applying to BNIT were (in addition to the general statements on
the training of staff included in the Policy Statement):
(i) Adequate staffing of the Agricultural Credit Division, to
be headed by an experienced and qualified agriculturist or
agricultural economist with administrative experience whose
appointment had to be made after consultation with the Bank;
(ii) Part B of the Project consisted of the provision of experts
to train and supervise the technical staff of the Agricultural
Credit Division of BNT;
1/   Special Fund for Agricultural Development/Special Fund for the
Encouragement of Fisheries.
2/   German Fund for Irrigation.
3/   Swedish International Development Agency.
4/   World Food Program.
5/   Special Agricultural Fund.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
(iii) To that effect, BN4T was to employ, in consultation with the
Bank, a farm management specialist, assisted by three experts
in loan evaluation and supervision.
Instead BNT decided not to involve the Agricultural Credit Division in manag-
ing the Project but to set up a separate special unit to appraise subprojects
and manage the implementation of the Project. The Unit was staffed with an
expatriate farm management expert, a project analyst acting as his counter-
part, three technical experts (farm mechanization, dairy farms, and irriga-
tion and date plantations) and several junior project analysts. The unit
also took charge of FSA loans. As a result, the Bank was no longer involved
with the Agricultural Credit Division, which was mostly in charge of short-
term credit, and, until recently, of supervising the CLCMs. The performance
of the technical experts in the Unit has ranged from adequate to excellent.
8.        In 1975, greater autonomy was given to branch managers to grant
short-term credits, within certain limits. Short-term credit is secured over
the relevant crop and is also protected by insurance against fire and hail.
Medium-term loans are secured by liens over stock and equipment, with legal
evidence of landownership. Long-term loans are secured by a mortgage on land.
Not all land is properly titled (see Annex 1), which in effect limits BNT
lending; with the progress made on land registration and the introduction of
new landownership certificates, however, this constraint can be expected to
decrease over the years. Supervision of other than Bank-financed loans is
insufficient, but the continuing provision of short-term loans to farmers
allows BN`T to keep in close contact with the financial situation of its bor-
rowers.
Operations
Short-term Loans
9.        BNT makes short-term loans from its own resources (see Table 2).
Agricultural short-term loans are made only to commercial farmers (mostly
in the North, see Annex 2); small-size farmers borrow from the CLCMs. Aver-
age short-term loan amounts are about D 1,600 for individual borrowers and
D 6,300 for cooperatives. The interest rate is 6% to 7%. Loan amounts are
subject to ceilings per ha which vary between D 10/ha for grain to D 270/ha
for off-season tomatoes.  Table 1 shows the amount of BNT short-term lending
and the ceiling per type of cultivation.
10.       The recent creation of the SCMs (Societes de Caution Mutuelle)--
mutual guarantee societies--will allow the smaller farmers to borrow collec-
tively from BNT, being jointly and severally liable. To qualify as a share-
holder of an SCM, a farmer must own a small-size farm, i.e. cultivating 20-
100 ha of grain or 1-4 ha of citrus or 2-10 ha of vineyards, or 1-5 ha of
fruits and vegetables, or 1-10 ha of fodder crops or 250-1,000 olive trees;
the owner of 5 ha of irrigated off-season fruits and vegetables may, at full
implementation of his project, reach a net annual income of up to D 3,000.
Thus this definition includes relatively large farmers and excludes small
ones who are nevertheless above the subsistence leve. The SCM loans are



ANNEX 3
Page 4
intended to be part of a credit and extension package, and will be mostly in
kind. These loans have to be guaranteed by the SCM and approved by a local
credit committee consisting of local representatives of the Ministries,of
Agriculture and Finance, of BNT, of the National Farmers Union, of the market-
ing agency involved (OC, ONH, etc.) and of the President of the SCM. As of
November 1975, 22 SCMs had been created and 19 more were in the process of
being established, mostly in the North, with an average membership of 200
members per SCM and an average capital of D 1,200. A general Guarantee Fund
would serve as a reserve for write-offs; its funds would come from a 2% in-
terest charge (the interest rate would be 6%,I 4% going to BNT and 2% to the
Guarantee Fund). Each SCM would also have its own provision for bad debts
funded by a commission additional to the interest charge. The loans which
are still unpaid two years after their disbursement (i.e, usually 12 to 15
months in arrears) would be written off as follows:
5% by BNT (provided the BNT representative to the local credit
committee did not vote against the loan)
25% by the SCM
70% by the Guarantee Fund (75% if BNT does not participate).
The SCM provision fund (including earnings on funds deposited by the SCMs)
shall be used to write off losses. However, the individual SOC shareholder
is responsible only to the extent of his share in the SCM (i.e, at least D 5
and, on the average, D 6) which is a limitation to the concept of mutual
liability. Because of bureaucratic slowness after the promulgation of the
law establishing SCMs (December 1973), no credits had been made to SCMs by
early Fall 1975, but they were expected to start soon thereafter.
11.       Short-term loans are made by BNT to industrial and commercial enter-
prises, and also to finance stocks of grain, olive oil and wine. These loans
are made to ONC and to three cooperatives (CCGC, COCEBLE, COSen) for grain,
to ONH for oil and to UCCV for wine. Their amount varies with the production
and the marketing possibilities (see Annex 2).
12.       BNT has made some medium- and long-term loans to industry and com-
merce on its own resources, but practically none to agriculture. BNlT's out-
standing medium- and long-term loans from own funds (excluding the counter-
part funds it provided under the first Bank project) have increased from
D 2.6 million in 1971 to D 8.9 million in 1974. However, commitments of
medium- and long-term loans for agriculture amounted only to negligible
amounts until 1974 (D 940 in 1972, D 2,100 in 1973, D 260 in 1974), but to
D 370,000 in 1975 (138 loans). Resources for medium- and long-term agricul-
tural credits have come from the budget (FOSDA/FOSEP), from bilateral aid
(FSA, FAI, SIDA) or from multilateral aid (PAM, IBRD/IDA) (see Table 2).
Total agricultural medium and long term lending amounted in 1975 to D 11
million (80% of which went to private farmers). Table 3 shows the break-
down of lending by loan size for each fund.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
13.       The FOSDA/FOSEP provide loans at low interest rates (2%-4.5%) and
direct investment grants. For each type of investment, the proportion of
self-financing, of grant and of loan, the maximum loan amount (per ha or per
tree), the repayment terms and the interest rates are specified (see Annex 2,
Table 6 for the present conditions and the proposed changes).
14.       The FSA fund is financed by a 1959 USAID loan of D 1.66 million to
Tunisia and additional credit for D I million between 1968 and 1971. This is
a revolving fund (with a 1% replenishment fee out of the 4% interest), which
is still disbursing.  Medium- and long-term loans are made to cooperatives
and to medium-size farmers with lending conditions usually the same as under
FOSDA. BNT carries one-fourth of the non-repayment risk out of its own
funds, the rest being charged to the FSA fund itself; repayments are rela-
tively adequate (811%). Under a free limit provision, loans above D 10,000
must be approved by the USAID mission in Tunis. The FAI fund is financed by
a 1968 German loan for D 676,000, of which D 170,000 were still available in
mid-1975. It is used only to finance well drilling and pumps. The interest
rate is 3% or 4%. The loans are appraised by a Project Unit (in the Ministry
of Agriculture) and approved by the Ministry of Agriculture; BNT does not
carry any risk (repayment 51%). The SIDA fund (D 1 million from Swedish Aid)
and D 200,000 of counterpart funds finance a development project of small
farms in Northern Tunisia. Short-and medium-term loans will be provided, in
conjunction with technical assistance to about 4,000 farmers near Le Kef and
Jendouba, most of whom farm less than 20 ha. Loans are usually in-kind and
interest rate is 3%. Four World Food Program (WFP) projects from 1967 to
date have financed, as part of a rural development project, a development
program of $32.5 million in Central Tunisia, mainly tree planting and sheep
grazing. This is, in fact, more a development grant than an agricultural
credit program (repayment 5%, but data not yet significant).
Equity Investments
15.       The equity portfolio of BN4T (see Table 5) has more than trebled
over the last five years from D 511,000 in 1971 to D 1,553,000 by September
1975. Investments are mostly in finance (43%) and in agroindustries (22%).
BIT has equity participation exceeding 20% of the capital in two companies,
the Societe Immobiliere et de Participations (SIMPAR) (40% of capital and
10.5% of BNT's portfolio), and the Societe Tuniso-Yougoslave de Developpement
(SOTYD) (25/% of capital and 0.5% of BNT's portfolio) since October 23, 1967
(despite a BCT norm and a Loan Agreement covenant to be enforced by December
31, 1972). The Societe Tunisienne de l'Industrie Laitiere (STIL) accounts
for about 13% of the whole portfolio. In addition to milk factories, STIL
manages date plantations, fruit and vegetable processing units, hotels and
department stores, maintaining very close ties with BNT, which owns 20% of
its equity capital.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
16.       Under the First Project, some covenants were specifically addressed
to the financial relationship between BNT and STIL (or other companies):
(i) The aggregate of lending 1/ and equity participation in any
single enterprise not to exceed (unless such excess is fully
guaranteed by the Government or a responsible financial in-
stitution):
- 25% of BNT's net worth (i.e. paid up equity
plus free reserves) 2/;
- 15% of BNT's net worth plus outstanding long-term
government loans;
- 50% of the total assets of the enterprise;
(ii)  The equity participation in any enterprise should not normally
exceed 20% of the capital of this enterprise (this is actually
a BCT regulation which is not very strictly enforced and was
a binding covenant to be enforced by December 31, 1972 for
STIL and SOTYD);
(iii) STIL accounts should be audited.
It is not yet possible to determine from data furnished by BNT if covenant (i)
was respected at all times; in the case of STIL, the 1975 audit of STIL should
provide adequate information.

Covenant (ii) was enforced on December 29, 1972 for STIL by reducing BNT's
participation from 28.8% to 20%; it has remained at this level since. How-
ever, there are still two companies, SOTYD and SIMPAR, in which BNT has a
participation greater than 20% (see para 15). As for covenant (iii), the
audit reports for 1969/70 (by Peat, Marwick & Mitchell) and 1970/71 (by Price
Waterhouse) (fiscal year ending September 30) have been received in the Bank
and the report for 1972/73 (by CdMA) was given to the Appraisal Mission.
Resources
17.       BNT classifies its resources into two categories, normal and spe-
cial resources. Normal resources, totalling D 109 million at end 1975 or
about 2/3 of the total, consist of:
(a)  Own Resources:  These include capital of D 4 million at end
1975 and free reserves (reserves and provision for miscel-
laneous risks) which have steadily grown due to BNT's regular
annual profit, to about D 6.2 million at end 1975 (from D 3.5
milion at end 1970).
(b)  Deposits:  Sight deposits (including special savings account)
increased from D 18 million at end 1970 to D 58.2 million at
end 1975, a more than threefold increase; term deposits (includ-
ing certificates of deposit) increased from D 7 million at end
1970 to D 18.5 million at end 1975. BNT collected between 14%
and 16% of all bank deposits in Tunisia until 1973, and 19% in
1974. To these should be added about D 5 million deposits by the
CLCMS. Deposits are collected both in the rural and urban sector,
from: the Government (and public agencies): 54%; from coopera-
tives and CLCMs: 13%; and from individuals: 33%.
(c)  Rediscount Facilities:  These are provided by BCT.  BNT was eli-
gible in 1973 and 1974 for D 17 million each year under the
regular ceiling, the medium-term ceiling (if BNT's use of re-
sources ratio is sufficient), the grain, olive oil and wine
ceilings and the "outside ceiling" rediscount for export bills,
i.e, 38% of total BCT rediscount at end 1973, and 33% at end
1974.
18.       BNT's special resources amounted to D 41.5 million used at end
1975, but totalled D 44.8 million in net commitments. They comprise two
types:
(a) BNT's Borrowings
(i) In 1961, BNT borrowed directly from USAID Development Loan
Fund (DLF), with the Government's guarantee, US$5 mil-
lion for 15 years with one year of grace. Full disburse-
ment was achieved by 1967. Interest was paid at 4% and
charged to subborrowers at 7%. Medium- and long-term
subloans were made for financing agricultural investments.



ANNEX 3
Page 8
(ii)  In 1971/72, BNT borrowed $5 million from IBRD (at 7.25%)
with Government's guarantee and $3 million from IDA through
the Government (at 1%), both loans being for 15 years
including 5 years of grace; the average rate was 4.9%
(plus commitment fee). The Loan/Credit will be fully
disbursed by September 1976 (Annex 4).
(iii) Special Agricultural Fund (Fonds Special Agricole FSA):
see para 14.
(b)  Managed Funds:  BNT administers several special funds made avail-
able to it by Government from its own budget or from external
aid. These funds are on-lent on concessionary terms in order
to stimulate the development of various agricultural subsectors.
The lending decision on subloans is either taken outside BNT,
sometimes with BNT having a say only as far as the credit-
worthiness of the borrower is concerned, or shared by BNT with
another agency. BNT does not usually assume any risk except
on funds originating from the Bank/IDA.
(i) In 1967, Tunisia received a Bank loan of US$12 million
at 6% and an IDA Credit of $6 million at 0.75% (484/99-TUN),
(reduced in November, 1970 to $6.1 million and $3.1 mil-
lion respectively) for the development of agricultural
production cooperatives in Northern Tunisia. Average cost
of funds to the Government was 4.25% (plus commitment fee),
but funds were passed to BNT at 4%. Subloans were made by
BNT for onlending at 6%,I/ i.e, with a 2% spread. BNT
assured 5% of the repayment risk on loans to cooperatives.
Full disbursement was achieved in early 1974.
(ii)  In 1971/72, BNT borrowed $1.83 million from IDA (270-TUN)
through the Government (plus $130,000 equivalent frcm the
Government) at 3% for 15 years including 5 years of grace,
with Government assuming 50% of any losses incurred by BNT
due to default by subborrowers. The Credit was to finance
through medium- and long-term loans the acquisition of fish-
ing boats and fishing gear by qualified fishermen. The Cre-
dit is expected to be fully disbursed by December 1977.
(iii) Debts consolidation (Apurement des Dettes, AD): not dis-
bursing any more.
1/   The rate for onlending agreed with the Bank was 8%, but a 2% rebate was
paid by Government.



ANNEX 3
Page 9
(iv)  Fund for the Development of Agricultural Production and
Service Cooperatives (Fonds pour le Developpement des
Unites de Production et des Cooperatives Agricoles de
Service, FODIUPCAS): not disbursing any more.
(v) Special Fund for Agricultural Development (Fonds Special
pour le Developpement Agricole, FOSDA): (see para 13).
(vi)  Special Fund for the Encouragement of Fisheries (Fonds
Special pour l'Encouragement de la Peche, FOSEP): similar
to FOSDA but for the fisheries sector.
(vii) German Fund for Irrigation (Fonds Allemand pour l'Irrigation,
FAI/FAIN): (see para 14).
(viii) Special Fund for Financial Assistance to Cooperatives (Fonds
Special pour les Amenagements Financiers, FOSAF): not dis-
bursing any more.
(ix) WJorld Food Program (WFP): (see para 14).
(x) Swedish International Development Agency Fund (SIDA):
(see para 14).
(xi)  Fund for the Protection of the Wine Industry (Fonds de
Defense de la Viticulture, DV): for D 150,000, has not
been used since its granting in 1962, due to lack of an
agreement between BNT and the Mlinistry of Agriculture.
Financial Position
19.       Profitability:  By careful administration of lending operations
from its own funds, BNT has been able to generate a sizable profit every year
(see Table 6: Income Statement). Gross income has been a low percentage of
average total assets (3.7% to 4.9%) as a result of the low interest rates
structure in Tunisia, but the cost of resources has also been very low (1.0%
to 1.5@' of average total assets), thus allowing for a reasonable spread (2.7%
to, 3.3' of average total assets, except in 1973: 3.8%). Allowance for pro-
visions has been small (0.3% to 0.6% of average total assets) and a sizable
provision for miscellaneous risks has been set up. Operating costs, includ-
ing depreciation, have been kept low (1.4% to 1.5% of average total assets,
and only 29% to 37% of gross income); since 1970, they have increased by 27%
per year on average (gross income having increased by 30% per year on aver-
age). Dividends and directors' fee were sizable, amounting in 1970-1972 to
between 31% and 34% of the net income distributed. The percentage decreased
to 19% in 1973 and 23% in 1974 because of the important allocation to the
tax-free reinvestment reserve (1972 incentives law). Net income as a per-
centage of year-end equity accounted for 24%-32%, except in 1973 when it



ANNEX 3
Page 10
reached 56%; dividends paid were 7% in 1970, 8% in 1971, 10% in 1972 and
1973, 8% in 1974 1/ and 9% in 1975. BNT started paying income tax in 1969
after a nine year exemption.
Forecasts
20.       BNT's forecasts of its flow of funds statement until 1978 have to
be considered as very tentative, with a margin of error higher than usual.
On this basis the mission attempted to derive forecasts of the BNT balance
sheet and income statement (see Table 9). The following assumptions have
been used for projections of the balance sheet until 1981:
(a) The equity capital increased to D 4 million in 1975 and
will reach D 6 million in 1980;
(b)  The reserves and provisions will increase by about 8% per year;
(c) Deposits will grow at about 10% per year;
(d) Short-term agricultural lending will grow at about 21% per year,
although the growth will be higher until 1977 and taper off later,
on the assumption that BNT's effort to reach small farmers will
be limited to the SCMs (accounting for about one-third of total
short-term agricultural lending), and that its lending to richer
farmers will reach saturation;
(e)  Medium-term agricultural lending from BNT's own funds, after a
rapid increase in 1975/76, will expand slowly due to the continu-
ing availability of cheaper FOSDA funds;
(f) Non-agricultural short-term lending will grow only at about
10%, due to increased competition from other banks, thus de-
creasing the proportion of short-term funds not going to agri-
culture from 90% to 80% of total short-term lending;
(g) Non-agricultural medium-term lending will grow at about 22%,
due to BNT's need to increase its medium-term lending to comply
with BCT regulations, and reluctance to do so in agriculture;
(h) IBRD resources and loans are projected on the basis of the last
progress and supervision reports on the existing projects
(Fisheries, Ag Credit I) and on reasonable disbursement assump-
tions for other projects (Irrigation Rehabilitation, Ag Credit
II); no other possible future projects have been taken into ac-
count;
1/   In 1974, 25% of the shares were issued in September and were not divi-
dend earnings; another 25% were created by incorporation of reserves and
distribution of one additional share per two shares outstanding, so that
thf diviLdend npsr share outstandincg as of early 197' was actual3y 12%.



ANNEX 3
Page 11
(i) FSA resources and loans will remain constant (replenishment
fee);
(j) FOSDA resources and lending will grow at about 27% per year;
(k) Total assets will increase by about 12.5% per year.
As for the income statement, it has been assumed that:
(a)  Gross income will increase from about 4.5% of total assets in
1974 to 5.4% in 1981, with a significant increase in 1976 due
to the new FOSDA lending terms;
(b)  The cost of resources wiLl increase from about 1.5% of total
assets in 1974 to 2.3% in 1981;
(c)  The operating costs (including depreciation) will increase very
slowly from 1.5% of total assets in 1974 to 1.7% in 1981;
(d)  Allocations to provisions will remain at 0.5% of total assets;
(e)  As a result, net income after tax will grow by about 10.5% per
year.
On the basis of these assumptions, BNT's financial condition is expected to
remain s-itisfictorv.
,1.       Financial Structure (see Table 7:  Balance Sheet):  BNT has to com-
ply with the financial ratios set by BCT (as described in Annex 2, Table 2).
Tts "use of resources" ratio was always well above the norm (15%):
1971      1972       1973       1974
18.0%     17.1%      28.1%      25.5%
Almost all of BNIT's medium-term loans are for industry and commerce or for
agricultural investments under the first Bank project. BNT does not seem
to have complied with the "development financing" ratio (30% until January
1975):
1970     1971      1972      1973      1974
15.5%    10.1%    10.2%    17.1%    13.0%
As a result, the missing amounts, which should have been invested in Govern-
ment bonds, are blocked by BCT. In 1970 the long-term debt equity ratio was
1:1, but the current ratio was 0.9:1, thus pointing to an unsatisfactory
liquidity position. Although precise data is not available, it can be esti-
mated, on the basis of the trend in the "solvency" ratio (see below), that
the debt/equity ratio has deteriorated after 1970 but improved in 1975 due to



ANNEX 3
Page 12
the increase in capital. However the Loan Agreement for the First Project
provided for the debt/equity ratio not to exceed 4:1, and this covenant seems
to have been respected, since BNT did not borrow large amounts long-term on
its own account.
22.       Other financial covenants were:
(a) Capital and reserves to be equivalent to at least 10% of sight
and fixed term deposits (BCT "solvency" ratio); in fact, the
ratio was:
1970    1971      1972      1973     1974      1975
10.2%   8.6%      7.7%      7.4%     6.8%      8.1%
(b)  Fixed assets, including participations not to exceed 75% of
paid-up equity plus free reserves; in fact, the ratio was:
1970    1971      1972      1973     1974      1975
39%     53%       63%       76%      63%       59%
(c)  BNT to have suitable protection against the foreign exchange
risk with respect to foreign loans.
It would seem that BNT was and still is under-capitalized, despite the recent
capital increase; the building up of reserves after 1970 did not keep pace
with the growth of deposits and lending. In a hypothetical case, if no divi-
dends above the statutory 3% dividend and no directors' fee had been distri-
buted, the ratio under (a) would have been only slightly better:
1970     1971      1972      1973     1974    1975
Amount of reserves (D'OQO)   967        1,257    1,578    1,967    2,723   2,963
Ratio                         10.2%      8.8%     8.1%      8.1%      8.4%    8.7%
The under-capitalization cannot, therefore, be totally attributed to the
dividend distribution policy, but also to the difference between the growth
rate of deposits and the growth rate of net income, thus pointing out the low
level of interest rates:
1971   1972   1973   1974   1975
Annual growth rate of deposits                  29%    22%    13%    60%       0%
Annual growth rate of net income                15%    17%    77%       0%    39%
Annual growth rate of capital plus reserves   9%         8%     9%    46%    37%
23.       Recoveries:  It is also difficult to evaluate the quality of the
BNT loan and investment portfolio and the adequacv of its provisions. Never-
theless, BNT's recovery procedures for loans made out of its own resources ap-
pear thorough and methodical, at least during the early stages (administrative



ANNEX 3
Page 13
procedure) where about 75% of the recoveries are made. If this approach does
not bring results, the loan is transferred to the litigation division of BNT,
which may go to court against the borrower; for the 2,100 or so files under
litigation (amounting to D 3.2 million, of which D 2 million out of BNT's own
funds), an additional 2% penalty interest rate is charged. Ilowever many old
loans on which there are no more repayment prospects are still carried on the
books and not written off, and, for some of them, no specific adequate provi-
sion has been made. The repayment rates are not very well known; they vary
-'Ldely depending on the source of funds and the risk BNT assumes, i.e, BNT's
Lncentive in allocating resources to recovery efforts. It seems that the re-
payment rate on loans out of government funds from 1956 till now has been
only about 50%. Table 8 shows some repayment data for 1975. Loans out of
IBRD/IDA funds were repaid at 92% (as of October 31, 1975) while loans out
of FSA were repaid at 81% and out of FOSDA (for similar investments) at 83%;
the average repayment rate for medium and long-term loans out of special re-
sources was 71% for individuals and 68% for cooperatives, while short-term
loans out of BNT's own funds were repaid at 77% for individuals and 92% for
cooperatives (all data as of October 31, 1975). The short-term loan repay-
ment rates will increase by the end of the marketing period (for 1973/74 they
were 91% for individuals and 93% for cooperatives).
24.       Portfolio:  Although it does not seem that the allocation of provi-
sions and the write-off of bad loans are in line with the recovery and litiga-
tion procedures, the provisions established by BNT are large and sufficient
to cover its risks: the provision for miscellaneous risks amounts to D 3.4
million and the provision for loans under litigation to D 1.1 million (re-
spectively, 40%, 45% and 35% of the loans under litigation in 1972, 1973 and
1974, but the increase in the provision over the previous year amounted to
73% of the increase in loans under litigation in 1973, but only to 23% in
1974). The provision for equity investments amounted to D 129,000 at end
1974; it had decreased from 20% of the equity investment portfolio in 1970
to 9% in 1973 and 11% in 1974, while the quality of the equity investment
portfolio had improved. The mere size of the provision for miscellaneous
risks provides a sufficient amount to cover BNT's own repayment risk, but
better management of the provision for loans under litigation would never-
theless be worthwhile and advisable. The reserve funds amounting to D 1 mil-
lion at end 1974 (legal and extraordinary reserves only) provide additional
protection.
25.       BNT's risk is linked to its exposure in the agricultural sector and
is therefore compounded by output fluctuations (bad harvests) and by political
or organizational difficulties in the agricultural sector (such as the coop-
eratives' movement). In fact, while BNT contributes 25.4% of total outstand-
ing loans by the banking sector (including arrears), at end September 1975,
its share in agriculture (half of BNT's outstanding loans and participations,
but only about 30% of the loans and participations on which BNT assumes a re-
payment risk) is 86.1% and in agroindustry 52.6%; its share in other industry
is 10.6%, in trade 36.9%, and in other services 10.5%.



ANNEX 3
Page 14
Accounts and Audit
26.       BNT has five levels of control:
(a) As a bank, BNT is subject to the usual Central Bank supervision;
(b) As a joint stock company, BNT is subject to the scrutiny of two
external comptrollers elected by the shareholders at the annual
general meeting (see Annex 2 para 4 (e));
(c) As a company with a State equity investment, BNT is subject
to the control of a financial comptroller appointed by the
Government and charged with the protection of Government's
interest in BNT;
(d) As a Bank/IDA borrower, BNT has to have its accounts audited
by independent auditors satisfactory to the Bank;
(e) BNT has its own internal inspection, which performs an adminis-
trative and performance control, but not a financial one.
27.       With the Bank's concurrence, BNT appointed Peat, Marwick & Mitchell
as its auditors in early 1971 for its 1970 accounts. The 1970 audit report
did not give an opinion on BNT's accounts and the Bank urged BNT to appoint
new auditors. Price W4aterhouse audited the 1971 accounts in collaboration
with a Tunisian firm, Cabinet d'Informatique, de Management et d'Audit (CIMA).
Starting with the 1972 accounts, CIMA (whose director is also one of BNT's
external comptrollers) did the audit alone, although this change was not
notified to the Bank and the audit reports were not sent to the Bank. The
audit report for FY1974 was received only in 1976 and the audit report for
FY1975 is not expected to be received before end 1976. The quality of those
audits prepared by CIMA is not satisfactory to the Bank and BNT was required
to correct the situation under the Project (see para 6.16). Audit reports
should be communicated to Board members for information, which is not the
present practice. At the same time, the financial statements submitted to
the Bank should be of a format identical to those submitted to the Central
Bank. BNT should also be encouraged to develop an internal financial audit
system.
B. Caisses Locales de Credit Mutuel
Institutional Aspects
28.       The Caisses Locales de Credit Mutuel (CLCMs) are credit cooperative
societies made up of farmers, craftsmen, shopkeepers and merchants. The
purpose of the CLCMs is stated as the promotion of savings and the provision
of short-term credit, mostly for productive purposes, under a limited mutual
guarantee system, to rural people facing difficulty in obtaining credit from



ANNEX 3
Page 15
the banking system. The CLCMs are governed by the Cooperative Law of 1967.
Membership is in principle open to any individual or company but admission is
subject to approval by the Board of Directors and subscription of a share (D
5) in the capital (as a result, capital is variable). Loans can be made only
to members, who must hold shares to the extent of 5%/ of their borrowings.
The joint liability of members is limited to their equity participation, thus
reducing the mutual liability aspect of the operations.
29.       Local credit sections were in operation from 1960 till 1966 as as-
sociations of BNT borrowers but without any well-defined financial role. At-
tempts were also made to create a Cooperative Bank which was taken over by
BNT in 1962. The first CLCMs were created in 1963 by the Government as part
of the objective to generalize mutual credit. During the 1962-64 Plan, a
program was drawn up to establish CLCMs in the Cap Bon region where small
fruit and vegetable farms are predominant. Nine CLCMs were created and re-
ceived a state grant of D 290,000 (the first five with D 50,000 each and the
last four with D 10,000 each). They were then totally independent from BNT
(although some BNT staff were seconded to them). By July 1965, there were
12 CLCMs, of which the most recently created did not receive any state grant.
Under agreements of May 1965 and January 1966 between BNT and the Planning
and National Economy Ministry, BNT becane entrusted with the management of
the CLCMs. The above-mentioned local credit sections of BNT were then abol-
ished.
30.       The main difficulties encountered by the CLGMs during their start-
ing period were:
(a)  Poor management by incompetent and inexperienced boards and
managers;
(b)  Inadequate assistance in terms of finance, technical guidance,
training and supervision by Government;
(c) Poor repayment performance because of borrowers' confusion be-
tween loans and grants.
As a result, the CLCMs were in a dismal financial position. BNT, when taking
over, attempted to correct this situation by seconding staff to the CLCMs,
training their staff, improving their administration, and especially their
financial management, and providing a rediscount facility to CLCM loans. BNT
was also supposed to prepare a development plan for the creation of new CLCMs
in the country. The situation did not improve much, however, and was further
aggravated by the cooperatives' situation and the poor harvests in 1967-69.
31.       Since 1970, the CLCMs have been regarded, on one hand, as remnants
of the cooperative era that should be abolished and, on the other hand, as
the only credit institutions in close contact with small farmers, and there-
fore worthy to be reorganized and developed. No clear policy on them has
emerged so far. There are now 43 CLCMs. BNT processes the accounting data
of the CLCMs and has a special division in charge of overseeing them.



ANNEX 3
Page 16
32.       All CLCMs are managed by a Board of 6 to 12 Directors who are
elected for three years by the members; the Directors are prominent farmers
or merchants. The Board is responsible for approving all loans upon the re-
commendation of the CLCM manager. Each CLCM is staffed by a manager, who is
a BNT staff member seconded to, and paid by, the CLC1I, and by between one and
four employees. A most important factor in the successful management of a
CLCM is the cooperation between the Board and the Manager, and the extent to
which the Board members are able and willing to approve loan applications on
the basis of banking criteria and not of patronage. The CLCM standard stat-
utes provided also for the establishment of a control committee elected by
the General Assembly; as members did not have the competence to carry out
these functions, no such committees seem to have been created in any CLCM.
Each CLCM has an average of 1,800 members.
33.       Most CLCMs are in the north, in the Sahel, and in the Cap Bon re-
gion and these are usually the most successful ones. At end 1974, 9 CLCMs
were in the Cap Bon (Nabeul), 11 in the Sahel (Sousse, Monastir, Mahdia) and
11 in the North (Tunis, Beja, Bizerte), accounting respectively for 49%, 13%
and 26% of total CLCM lending. Of the 9 CLCMs in the Cap Bon, 8 were making
a profit in 1974, compared to 2 out of 11 in the Sahel, 4 out of 11 in the
North, and 8 out of 12 in the rest of the country. The Cap Bon area is the
only region where mutual credit has worked well, partially for socio-cultural
reasons and partially because of the existing economic structure with a great
number of relatively small farmers deriving an adequate income from the
cultivation of fruit, vegetables and citrus.
34.       The CLCMs perform essentially two functions, the provision of bank-
ing services and short-term lending. Due to their location in small cities
where there is often no other bank, they can attract fairly sizable deposits
from merchants, farmers and cooperatives. However, only the richer members
have been able to deposit significant amounts and the interest rates paid
(fixed by law) are very low (see Annex 2, Table 1). The CLCMs most success-
ful in attracting deposits are those not suffering from competition from
another bank, usually BNT (16 CLCMs are in a city where there is also a BNT
branch) or those close to cooperatives having large amounts of liquidity.
The CLCMs account for about 2% of all bank deposits in Tunisia. An important
part of CLCMs' income comes from other banking transactions, especially for-
eign exchange transactions (usually with tourists).
Short-term Loans
35.       The CLCMs provide only short-term credit.  Fewer and fewer CLCM
loans are for agriculture and more and more for commerce. The norms govern-
ing the size of the loans to agriculture and their repayment schedule are the
same as for BNT (see Table 1) but loans cannot normally exceed D 500. Loan
appraisals are usually not very thorough and the feasibility of the proposed
cropping pattern is rarely examined. Collaboration with the local staff of
the Ministry of Agriculture is minimal. The interest rate is 6% (7% for non-
agricultural loans) with an additional 1% paid into a general reserve, thus



ANNEX 3
Page 17
making credit more expensive for CLCM borrowers than for BNT borrowers. Ad-
ditional fees may bring the cost of funds to about 8-9%. In addition, bor-
rowers must hold shares to the extent of 5% of their borrowings and since the
shares are not earning dividends, this represents an additional cost. No
subsidies or grants are made available to CLCM borrowers. The collateral re-
quired is the unlimited liability of the borrower to the CLGM, including his
crop and his real and chattel property. However the joint liability of CLCM
members to others (State, BNT) is limited to the amount of capital shares
owned by each member.
36.       Table 12 shows the amount lent by the CLCMs.  Only in 1969 were
loans made to cooperatives; in 1973, after only 34% had been repaid, the
State repaid the outstanding amount to the CLCMs. The average loan amount
almost doubled in real terms between 1966 and 1974. Only a small proportion
of the members are actually borrowers, at most 10% in 1974, since many have
no interest in the CLCMs and reluctantly became members because of political
pressures. The CLCMs represent a major part of the total agricultural short-
term lending only in the Cap Bon (80%) and in the Sahel (73%) (about 37% for
the country as a whole). However, the CLCMs lend more and more outside agri-
culture (from 9% of their assets in 1970 to 18% in 1974) and less to agricul-
ture (from 38% of assets to 21%).
Resources
37.      'Most of the CLCM resources come from deposits, usually sight depos-
lts _- ,4 million at endi 1974) ana fro.. their equity capital and free re-
serve (D 1 million) the rates paid on deposits are those prescribed by the
Central Bank (Annex 2, Table 1). Since 1971, BNT does not rediscount CLCM
loans (the BNT rediscount rate was 5%, i.e., 1% more than the BCT discount
rate for BNT at the time). Except in 1969, because of the loans to coopera-
tives, the rediscount facility never exceeded D 300,000 (see Table 12). The
State has provided only D 290,000 as a grant to nine of the first CLCMs, and,
asile from its repayment of the outstanding loans to cooperatives in 1972
(D I million), has not provided any other funds to the CLCMs.
Financial Position
38.       Profitability:  The financial spread available to the CLats (3.4%
to 3.9,' of average total assets) is spent almost entirely on operating costs,
which are quite stable at 3.2% of average total assets; this proportion is
not unduly high for a small farmers' decentralized credit system. As a re-
sult, the average loss ranges from 0.4% to 0.8% of average total assets for
the whole CLCM system. The income from loans to agriculture has decreased
from 20% of total income in 1971 to 15% in 1974, while the income from other
loans more than doubled from 14% to 29% of total; the income received from
BNT (deposits and share capital) was quite stable, fluctuating between 34%
and 38% of total income, while the other income decreased from 32% to 21%.
The increase in the volume of non-agricultural loans and, as a result, in
income from them, was a clear consequence of the relative profitability of



ANNEX 3
Page 18
lending to agriculture (2.7% to 3.2% net yield) and to other sectors (8.1%
to 9.2% net yield) this being mostly a result of the substantial arrears on
agricultural loans; the net yield on assets with BNT remained around 3.5%,
and the yield on the other assets decreased from 9.1% to 7% (still quite
large, mostly because of the profit made on banking transactions). The aver-
age yield on assets varied between 4.4% and 4.8% during the period 1971-74
(see Table 10).
39.       Financial Structure.  The CLCMs have neither long-term debt nor
long-term lending but their sizable bad debts hamper their liquidity. Their
capital base is very small and they are undercapitalized for the amount of
risk on loans they have to support. A sizable portion of the CLCM assets
(50% in 1972, down to 40% in 1974) is actually not used for lending but kept
with BNT in low yielding (about 3%) accounts and as equity participation; the
CLCMs have been a source of cheap funds (net yield about 3%) to BNT, instead
of using these funds for their lending operations. The main financial pro-
blem of the CLCMs is the size of their arrears which accounted for 28% of
total assets in 1970, down to 15% in 1974 (see Table 11).
40.       Recoveries:  Because of inadequate recovery procedures, an import-
ant portion of CLCM loans has never been repaid: the annual repayment rate
is barely over 50%, and arrears account for half of the portfolio (1971 -
57%; 1972 - 50%). The situation has improved somewhat since 1972 (1974 - 35%
of the portfolio in arrears) and more adequate provisions, though still in-
sufficient, have been made (from 8% of arrears in 1970 to 20% in 1974). The
situation is, however, very unsatisfactory and cannot be expected to improve
much as long as the old arrears (before 1970) are not written off.

ANNEX 4

APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
TUNISIA
Performance Under the First Agricultural Credit Project
(Credit/Loan 263/779-TUN)
A. Project Data and Description
Project Data
1.   Credit Amount                  US$3.0 million
Loan Amount                   US$5.0 million
Loan Terms                     15 years including 5 years of grace
at 7 1/4 percent p.a.
Project Cost                  US$15.0 million
Foreign Exchange Cost         US$8.0 million
Date of Loan Agreement        7/12/71
Date of Effectiveness          1/3/72
Closing Date (original)       9/30/75
Closing Date (actual)         12/31/76
Amount Disbursed as of
October 1, 1976             US$7.4 million
Amount committed as of
October 1, 1976             US$0.6 million
Project Description
2.        The Credit/Loan was made to finance part of Banque Nationale de
Tunisie (BNT) lending to commercial farmers for investment in grain farm
mechanization and dairy farm development in Northern Tunisia, and for date
palm plantations to be undertaken by the Societe Tunisienne de l'Industrie
Laitiere (STIL) in the desert areas of Southwestern Tunisia. The Project



ANNEX 4
Page Z
included financing of technical assistance to strengthen BNT's loan ap-
praisal division, plantation management of STII. and vehicles for technical
services.
3.        The approximate estimate made during appraisal as to the number
and size of subprojects to be financed under the Project were as follows:
(a) 460 grain farms, each about 100 ha surface, investing in
wheel tractors and farm machinery;
(b) 130 grain farms, each at least 200 ha of crops, investing
in crawler tractors and farm machinery;
(c) 240 grain farms each at least 200 ha of crops, investing in
harvesters and farm machinery;
(d) 150 dairy farms, each with at least 8 ha irrigated land; and
(e) 18 date palm plantations each of about 50 ha, to be irrigated
by artesian wells (to be implemented by one subborrower STIL).



ANNEX 4
Page 3
4.        The Project cost estimates were:
US$ ('000)               Foreign
Local    Foreign    Total       Exchange
1.   Grain Farms
Wheel tractors                          930      1,680     2,610          65
Crawler tractors                        740      1,340     2,080          65
Combines                              1,230      2,240     3,470          65
Attachments                             300        740      1,040         65
Subtotal                           3,200      6,000      9,200         65
2.   Dairy Farms
Heifers in-calf:
local                                 560       -          560          -
imported                             -          350        350        100 L
Water supply development                360        520        880         60
Barn improvement                        170         60        230         25
Minor equipment                          20         50         70         70
Subtotal                           1,110        980      2,090         47
3.   Date-Palm Plantations
Irrigation, drainage, land
preparation, and planting          2,450        810      3,260         25
Total                              6,760      7,790    14,550          53
Support Services
Expert services                          30        180        210         86
Dairy extension services                200        -          200          -
Vehicles for extension staff
and BNT                                10         30        40         75
Subtotal                              240        210       450         47
Grand Total                         7,000     8,000    15,000          53
5.        The loan categories were originally allocated, each according to
its foreign exchange component; however, Bank's disbursement was in all cases
to be uniformly 75% of BNT loans disbursed. The result of this oversight was
that the Date Palm Plantations Category, which had the smallest foreign ex-
change component, had to be increased from US$0.81 million to US$1.7 million
in order to cover the 52.5% of investment as set up in the overall financing
plan. The overall categories have been adjusted accordingly, so that the
final schedule of withdrawals of the proceeds of the Loan/Credit was:



ANNEX 4
Page 4
A.   Grain Farms                     US$5.00 million
B.   Dairy Farms                     US$1.10 million
C.   Date Palm Plantations           US$1.70 million
D.   Technical Assistance            US$0.20 million
Total                     US$8.00 million
6.        The Loan/Credit was expected to be disbursed over a period of 3
years and 3/4, with disbursements starting in second quarter FY72.
7.        The financial rate of return of the subprojects was expected to
range from 14% to 20% and the economic rate of return of the whole Project
was estimated at 22%.
B. Project Implementation
8.        The Project became effective more than six months after the signing
date, due to delays in fulfillment of two effectiveness conditions, namely
(a) the ratification by the National Assembly of the Loan/Credit documents
and (b) setting up of a Technical Unit of four experts within BNT. In 1972
the Project appeared to be off to a good start. The demand for loans under
the mechanization component remained satisfactory through 1972, although the
average loan amounts were somewhat lower than expected. The same phenomenon,
only more pronounced, was observed in dairy loans. The successive supervi-
sion missions repeatedly called Government's attention to this question and
several letters in this respect were addressed to Government. The Date Palm
Plantation component, although somewhat behind schedule, continued to be im-
plemented satisfactorily.
9.        During 1973 loan disbursements for farm mechanization decreased
sharply.  This was mainly due to severe floods in March 1973 which destroyed
20-25%, of the cereal crop in the Project area. The demand for loans improved
later in the year, but remained much under the projected level. Dairy farm
loan applications and disbursements also remained far behind schedule. The
discrepancy with appraisal estimates did not reside so much in the number of
loans made but rather in the average amount of investment financed (D 2,220
instead of D 5,110). The structure of investment made differed substan-
tially from the structure anticipated during appraisal. The average number
of heifers financed by farm was 8.5 instead of 12 as anticipated, and the
irrigation network and equipment which were expected to represent about 42%
of the investment in each loan actually amounted to only 3% of the loan
amounts. Moreover, the cost of imported dairy cows rose from D 300 to D 420-
450 while the price of milk remained unchanged, making it hardly profitable
for farmers to invest in imported dairy cows. Finally the question of com-
peting FOSDA funds was still not resolved and BNT continued disbursement of
these funds to farmers who could qualify under the Project. The implementa-
tion of the Date Palm Plantation component of the Project remained satis-
factory.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
10.       The loan disbursement situation remained much the same through
1974. In the case of the Farm Mechanization component, however, the number
of incoming loan applications and loan approvals increased sharply in the
second half of the year mainly as a result of a Government/BNT decision to
exclude all FOSDA funds from farm mechanization and of lack of new American
AID funds previously also used by BNT to finance this category of subprojects.
In order to speed up disbursements under the Dairy Farm Mechanization, Bank
agreed on July 19, 1974, to BNT's request to include the financing of a Milk
Processing Plant to be built by STIL in the Sousse Region.
C. Project Realization
Investment in Grain Farms
11.       In all about 830 loans were expected to be disbursed under the cat-
egory directly affecting grain production on at least 12,000 ha. The aggre-
gate loan amount to be disbursed by BNT under this heading was expected to be
D 3.4 million (US$6.4 million at the then prevailing rate) resulting in an
average subloan amount of D 4,096. As of June 30, 1976, BNT received under
this category 866 loan applications, of which 693 were approved, 109 were
rejected and 55 were under consideration; and 596 loans were disbursed. The
total loan amount involved for the 693 approved loans amounted to D 2.8 mil-
lion resulting in an average subloan amount of D 4,000, remarkably near to
the original estimates.
12.       The average prices paid for harvester and crawler tractors appear
to be very near to those projected. The more significant difference in wheel
tractor prices may be due to larger tractors purchased than expected during
appraisal.
Investment in Dairy Farm Development
13.       About 150 subloans were expected to be disbursed under this cate-
gory involving purchase of about 1,800 heifers, investment in irrigation on
about 1,200 ha, investment in barn improvement, and purchase of miscellaneous
forage and hay-making machinery. Total investment was estimated at D 1.1
million and BNT disbursements at D 0.77 million resulting in an average sub-
loan amount of D 5,110. As of June 30, 1976, BNT received 256 subloan appli-
cations under this heading of which 138 were approved and 118 rejected. Only
99 subloans were disbursed.l/ The total loan amount involved for the 138
loans approved was D 409,000 (without taking into account the loan for the
1/ Including 1 loan for the milk processing plant at Sidi Bou Ali amounting
to D 159,000.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
milk processing plant), the average subloan amount was D 2,963. The pur-
chased 908 heifers include 500 imported pure breed heifers (average price
D 340), the rest were merely exclusively Tunisian born pure breed heifers
(average price D 304).
Date Palm Plantations
14.       All the new plantations under the Project were to be executed by
STIL, a Government controlled holding already owning and/or directly admin-
istering at the time of Project appraisal some 500 ha of modern date planta-
tions. It was estimated that some 18 artesian boreholes will be financed in
the Djerid and Nefzaoua regions of Southwest Tunisia. The Project was also
to cover the necessary irrigation and drainage networks, land levelling and
preparation, purchase and plantation of date palms and windbreaks, construc-
tion of storage and packaging facilities and purchase of tractors with
trawlers. The total cost of the subproject was estimated at D 1.7 million.
As of June 30, 1976, STIL's investments amounted to D 1.58 million, however,
the final figure was expected to reach and somewhat exceed the appraisal
estimate of D 1.71 million. Remaining expenses will cover levelling, soil
preparation, irrigation network of an additional 73 ha, construction of 7
small transit storage facilities and some planting of replacements on the
newly established plantations. The completed Project will therefore cover
a group of 9 plantations (some of them interconnected), of which 4 are sit-
uated in the Nefzaoua area (South of Chot Djerid) and 5 in the Djerid area
(North of the Chot).

APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
TUNISIA
Representative Models
Small Farms
1.        Models No. 1 through 4 (No. 1:  5 ha Fruit and Vegetable Farm, No.
2: 5 ha Vegetable-Livestock-Forrage Farm, No. 3: 10 ha Grain Farm, No. 4:
10 ha Mixed farming) are farms which would qualify under the new FOSDA regu-
lations for concessionary credit. Models 1 and 2 represent farms of 5 ha
located near Tunis or another larger urban center most likely in Northern
Tunisia. PreProject situation of both farms is similar: 2 ha of the farms
are left fallow, 1 ha is cultivated with fruit and vegetables, 2 ha are under
wheat. The existing well is shallow and available water is barely sufficient
for irrigation of 1 ha. Tractor services for ploughing are supplied by and
payable to SONAMO. Pre-Project family farm income is D 320, or D 53 per
capita.
2.        In Model 1 the farmer's investment would consist in deepening and
improving his well, in purchase of pipes for irrigation and in the purchase
of a cultivator. At full development he would cultivate 4 ha of fruit and
vegetables and one ha of alfalfa; all of his production would be sold. The
investment amounting to D 3,255, would be financed as follows: 70% BNT loan,
10% Government grant and 20% own contribution. The financial rate of return
of the investment would be over 50%, due both to the extension of irrigation
and to the much more intensive use of agriculture inputs. At full implementa-
tion (year 3), the net farm income (before loan repayment) would be D 2,185
or D 364 per farmer's family member.
3.        In Model 2 the farmer would complement same investments as in Model
I by the purchase of 10 crossbreed in-calf heifers, and the construction of
a stable. Produced fodder would be wholly used but would not be sufficient
to feed the livestock, so additional fodder would be purchased. The total
investment would amount to D 8,505 and would be financed as follows: BNT
loan 65%, Government grant 15%, and 20% farmer's own contribution. The fi-
nancial rate of return of the investment would be 30%. At full implementa-
tion (year 4), the net farm income (before loan repayment) would be D 2,109
or D 351 per farmer's family member.
4.        Model 3 represents a farm of 10 ha in the grain producing regions
of Tunisia with no possibility of irrigation. Before Project half of the
farm surface is left fallow, 1 ha is planted with olive trees and 4 ha cul-
tivated with grain. The farmer rents tractor and harvesting services from



ANNEX 5
Page 2
SONAMO. The pre-Project farm income is, at, D 87 extremely low and would not
sustain a family. The Project investment is limited to the purchase of two
draft animals and few basic agriculture implements. At full implementation,
1 ha of land would be cultivated under fodder crops to feed the draft animals.
The farmer would continue to rent harvesting services from SONAMO but would
not need to rent a tractor for ploughing. More intensive and timely use of
fertilizer and pesticides would be condition of success of the subproject.
The total investment would amount to D 1,470 and would be financed as follows:
65% BNT-loan, 15% Government grant and 20% farmers contribution. Financial
rate of return of the subproject would be 18%. At full implementation (year
3) the net farm income would be D 442 or D 74 per farmer's family member.
5.        Model 4 represents a 10 ha farm near Tunis or another large urban
center. Before Project 1 ha is cultivated with fruit and vegetables, 4 ha
with grain, I ha is planted with olives and 4 ha are left fallow. SONAI4O is
supplying tractor services. Pre-Project farm income would be D 382 (D 64
per farmer's family member). The investment would consist of well improve-
ments, purchase of a pump, extension of irrigation network, purchase of 10
in-calf heifers and 2 draft animals, and of the construction of a stable. At
full development the farmer would cultivate 2 ha with fruit and vegetables,
5 ha with irrigated alfalfa, he would maintain 1 ha under grain, I ha olive
grove, and seed legumes on the remaining 1 ha. Produced fodder would be
wholly used, but would not be sufficient to feed his livestock, so additional
forage and concentrate would be purchased. Intensive use of farm inputs
would be part of the Project. The total investment would amount to D 8,925
which would be financed as follows: 65% BNT loan, 15% Government grant and
20% borrowers contribution. Financial rate of return of the investment
would be 15%. At full implementation (year 4), the net farm income would be
D 1,577 (D 263 per farmer's family member).
Commercial Farms
6.        Models 5 and 6 are larger farms which would not qualify for con-
cessional credit. Model 5 represents a 100 ha farm without access to irrig-
ation. Before Project 25 ha are cultivated under wheat, 10 ha under legumes
and 15 ha under fodder. Net pre-Project farm income is D 1,870 (D 312 per
family member). The Project would consist of combining grain farming with
dairy farming. At full implementation, fodder would be cultivated on 40 ha,
wheat on 50 ha and legumes on 10 ha. Investment would consist in the pur-
chase of 10 in-calf heifers, the construction of a stable and of an ensilage
pit. The total investment cost would amount to D 8,925. BNT's loan would
cover 70% of the investment and the farmer would contribute 30%. The finan-
cial rate of return would be 28%. At full development (year 4), net farm
income would be D 5,940 (D 990 per farmer's family member).
7.        Model 6 represents a grain farm of 150 ha.  Before Project, 40 ha
are cultivated under wheat, 20 ha are cultivated under barley, 30 ha are



ANNEX 5
Page 3
an olive grove and 60 ha are left fallow. Harvesting and tractor services
are rented from SONAMO. Pre-Project farm income is D 2,256 (D 376 per family
member). The Project would essentially consist of mechanization in order to
cultivate the whole farm area and in order to dispense of SONAMO tractor
services. Investment would consist of the purchase of a tractor and of prin-
cipal agriculture machinery such as plough, seeder, cultivator and trailer.
At full implementation, 50 ha would be under wheat, 40 ha under barley, 30 ha
cultivated fallow, and 30 ha would remain under olives. The total investment
cost would amount to D 7,665 of which BNT loan would cover 70% and farmer's
contribution 30%. Financial rate of return of the subproject would be 34%. -
At full development (year 3), net farm income would be D 4,577 (D 763 per
farmer's family member).
Date Palm Plantations - Model 7
8.        The Project would finance investment in development of 4 date palm
plantations of between 50 and 75 ha each in the Djerid and/or Netfzaoua areas.
The Djerid wells, usually about 650m deep, present on average an,a,Iesian
yield of over 51 1/sec. By pumping the yield can nearly be doubled, and pro-
vides therefore ample supply of water for a plantation of 75 ha. In the
Nefzaoua area the underground water level is at about 200 m and th1- artesian
yields are around 30 1/sec. Thus with pumping, the economical size of planta-
tions is about 50 ha. The final surface of each plantation in both areas
will depend on the effective yields of the new wells. It is expected that
the SCMV investing in Plantation development would have about 50-75 members
(actual membership of the SCMVs now being formed) and one plantation of 75
members would therefore directly benefit some 450 people.
9.         Investment would be in deep well drilling, land leveling and devel-
opment, irrigation and drainage networks, purchase of a tractor and miscel-
laneous agricultural equipment, purchase of young palm trees and windbreakers
and, at the beginning of the cropping period (7th year), construction of a
pumping station and of a storage building. The maintenance of the plantation
during the unproductive period is also to be added to the investment cost.
The total investment for a plantation of 75 ha in the Djerid region (Model)
is estimated at D 263,300 (D 3,510 per ha). The financial rate of return
would be about 11%. Investment in plantations in the Nefzaoua area, due to
higher underground water level, would be lower (in absolute and also per ha
figures) and consequently have a higher rate of return. The expected net
income per farmer (Djerid plantation) family at full development (year 15)
would be D 1,504 or per family member D 250.
Olive Oil Mills - Model 8
10.       The Project would aim at diminishing the regional shortage in olive-
crushing capacity in the under-equipped governorates, partictilatly in the gov-
ernorates of Jendouba, Beja and Kasserine, by financing                  nt of 18
new olive oil mills each of 30 tons/day crushing capacity.           e  18 oil mills
would be in addition to the approximately 15 mills that will be financed (on



ANNEX 5
Page 4
the basis of the present rate of 6 to 8 units yearly) by the BNT from other
funds over the next 2 years.
11.       The establishment of oil mills near the plantations would reduce
transportation costs and losses. The modern equipment installed in the mills
would produce high-quality oil (with low acid content) which would bring
higher prices and would help to increase exports and diversify foreign markets.
The oil mills would have equipment of the discontinuous type for the tradition-
al sector (small order processing) or of the continuous type for those pro-
ducers who process their own output or purchase olive harvests. Each of the
oil mills would be provided with storage capacity (financed by ONH) necessary
to store 50%0 of the expected annual production i.e. about 150 tons.
12.       The investment cost per mill is estimated at D 99,700 of which BNT
loan would finance 63%, ONH loan 7% (67% of the cost of storage), and sub-
borrowers contribution 30%. Financial rate of return of the investment would
be about 29%.

ANNEX 8
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
TUNISIA
Economic Rate of Return of the Project
1.        The economic rate of return was calculated by aggregating the rep-
resentative models as described in Annex 5 and adding costs for administering
the Project.
2.        The economic rate of return calculations are based on the following
assumptions:
(a)  Output Prices.  For wheat, barley, and beef the Bank's commod-
ity world price projections (in constant 1975 prices) were used
as basic data. The prices of milk and of livestock have been
projected to develop according to the beef price. The remain-
ing products have been priced at prevailing farmgate prices.
(b)  Input Prices.  Investment items have been priced at constant
end 1975 prices net of import duties and taxes.  Prices of
fertilizer and feed grains have been projected on the basis
of the development of the Bank's commodity world price esti-
mates. Prices of replacement cattle have been projected to
change according to the beef price. Other inputs have been
valued at end 1975 farmgate prices, adjusted by taxes where
applicable.
(c)  Labor.  Family labor has been costed at 50% of operating
expenses because some employment opportunities for family
labor are available. Hired labor has been valued at the
prevailing wage rates.
(d)  Olive Oil Mills Benefits.  Calculation of benefits from
olive oil mill investments has been based on the value
added by processing the olives into oil. Price series in-
dicate a strong correlation between the price of olives
and the price of olive oil; in the absence of projections
of olive oil prices, financial prices have been used.
(e)  Administrative Costs.  Costs have been based on salaries of
additional staff to be hired by BNT.
(f) Exchange Rate. The exchange of $2.40 per D 1 has been used
for all calculations.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
3.        Based on these assumptions, the project benefits and costs have
been calculated as shown in the attached table. The Project's economic rate
of return would be about 34%. The sensitivity analysis shows the following
results:
Investment Costs                +10%:        29%
Operating Costs                 +10%:        29%
Benefits                        -10%         23%
4.        The economic rate of return indicates that the Project would result
in substantial gains to the economy.
